The Invalidity of Arguing by Absence of Proof
Dispute over using absence of evidence as proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on using absence of proof as evidence and explain why the majority rejects it.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence in Hanafi usul.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul principle: can a mujtahid argue that a ruling does not exist simply because he cannot find evidence for it? The text presents the dispute, showing that while some allow it based on a Quranic verse, the majority of Hanafis hold that absence of proof does not constitute proof. Understanding this principle is crucial for accurate istinbat and avoiding fallacious reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the first sentence: what does it mean to argue by absence of proof? It is using the lack of evidence to support a claim of negation or affirmation.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
The author distinguishes between a subjective claim (stating personal lack of knowledge) and an objective claim (stating non-existence in reality). The first is permissible, the second is disputed.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Some scholars cite Quran 6:145 where the Prophet says 'I do not find ...' as evidence that absence of evidence can indicate permissibility. Note the verse's context.
The verse: 'Say, I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden...'
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The majority respond that the verse reflects the Prophet's personal knowledge, not a general principle. They argue that absence of evidence does not entail actual absence, and that the Quran commands 'Bring your proof' (2:111).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? A mujtahid cannot definitively conclude a ruling's non-existence from lack of evidence; he can only state his lack of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتجاج بلا دليل لأجل النفي بأن يقول هذا الحكم غير ثابت؛ لأنه لا دليل عليه، فإن ادعى أنه غير ثابت في ذهن المستدلّ فلا شك في جوازه؛ لأن عدم وجدانه الدليل يقتضي عدم وجدانه أي المستدل الحكم في علمه، وإن ادعى أنه غير ثابت في نفس الأمر لعدم وجدان الدليل عليه فاختلفوا فيه؛ فقيل: هو جائز لقوله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ لا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَيَّ مُحَرَّمَا﴾ الآية، (الأنعام: ١٤٥) فإنه تعالى علم نبيه الاحتجاج بلا أجد دليلاً على عدم حرمته، وقيل: جائز في الشرعيات دون العقليات؛ لأن مدّعي النفي والإثبات في العقليات مدعي حقيقة الوجود والعدم، فلا بد له من دليل، ولا يكفي عدم الدليل بخلاف الشرعيات؛ فإنها ليست كذلك، وعند الجمهور ليس بحجة أصلاً، لا في النفي ولا في الإثبات.
Argument by absence of proof is for negation: one says 'this ruling is not established because there is no evidence for it.' If he claims it is not established in the mind of the researcher, there is no doubt it is permissible, because his not finding evidence implies his not finding the ruling in his knowledge. But if he claims it is not established in reality due to lack of evidence, they differed: some said it is permissible, based on His saying 'Say, I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden...' (6:145), for Allah taught His Prophet to argue by 'I do not find' as evidence for absence of prohibition. And some said it is permissible in legal matters but not rational, because the claimant of negation or affirmation in rational matters claims actual existence or non-existence, so he must provide evidence, and absence of evidence is not sufficient, unlike legal matters for they are not so. And according to the majority it is not a proof at all, neither for negation nor for affirmation.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage addresses the usul principle of al-ihtijaj bi-la dalil—using the absence of evidence to prove a legal ruling. The author explains two interpretations: if the claimant merely states he personally found no evidence, it is acceptable as a statement of personal knowledge. However, if he claims the ruling has no existence in reality because he found no evidence, the scholars differ. Some allow it, citing the Quranic verse where the Prophet (peace be upon him) was taught to say 'I do not find' as evidence. Others distinguish between rational and legal matters. The majority (jumhur) among our Hanafi scholars and the Shafi'is hold that it is not a proof at all, since lack of evidence does not entail actual absence. This is a key point in usul: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

Key Terms
الاحتجاج بلا دليل (al-ihtijāj bi-lā dalīl): Using the absence of evidence as a proof for the non-existence of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (jumhur) of Hanafis, what is the ruling on using the absence of evidence to prove a legal ruling?
A) It is permissible in both legal and rational matters.
B) It is permissible only in legal matters.
C) It is not a valid proof at all.
D) It is valid only for negation.

Key Takeaway
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence in Hanafi usul; a ruling cannot be proven or disproven by the lack of dalil alone.

Three Categories Fixed by Text, Not Qiyas
Explaining three categories fixed by nass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories (shart, wasf al-shart, hukm) that require nass and apply the Hanafi rule that qiyas cannot establish them.

Core Idea
Conditions, their descriptions, and rulings are fixed by nass; qiyas cannot create or negate them.

This passage from al-Nur al-Anwar teaches that certain foundational elements of Shari'ah—conditions (shurut), descriptions of conditions, and rulings (ahkam)—must be established directly from the Qur'an and Sunnah, not through analogical reasoning (qiyas). Using the example of marriage witnesses, the author contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Malik and al-Shafi'i, and then applies the same principle to the case of a one-rak'ah prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Locate the three distinct elements discussed: shart (condition), wasf al-shart (description of condition), and hukm (ruling). Each is exemplified by a specific fiqh issue.
The examples are: witnesses (shart), 'adalah/dhukurah (wasf al-shart), and al-batira' prayer (hukm).
2
Examine the Hanafi proof for each
For each category, note the specific nass (text) the Hanafi school relies on and how it is interpreted to exclude any additions or modifications.
For witnesses: 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' (mutlaq). For description: the absolute nature indicates no extra conditions. For hukm: prohibition of al-batira' by explicit hadith.
3
Analyze the opposing views
Identify the Malik and Shafi'i positions and their dalil. Note how the Hanafi response counters each using principles of usul al-fiqh.
Malik: hadith about announcement. Shafi'i: hadith with wali and 'adalah, plus qiyas on hadd.
4
Apply the principle
Conclude that conditions, descriptions, and rulings cannot be derived by qiyas; they require explicit nass. This is a fundamental Hanafi usul rule.
The rule applies even if a qiyas seems plausible; the text takes precedence.

Source Text

Chapter of Analogy. 48. His saying the Exalted: 'Take from their wealth a charity to purify them and cleanse them thereby' (At-Tawbah: 103). Explanation of the categories of what is established by causal reasoning. And witnesses in marriage are an example of a condition; for witnesses are a condition in marriage, and it is not permissible to speak about that, i.e., about the conclusion of the marriage, establishing this condition by opinion and cause. Rather, we establish it by his saying: 'No marriage except with witnesses.' And Malik said: He does not require witnessing in it but rather publicity, due to his saying: 'Announce marriage and even with the duff.' And the condition of uprightness ('adalah) and masculinity (dhukurah) in them, i.e., in the witnesses of marriage, is an example of establishing a description of a condition. For witnesses are a condition, and uprightness and masculinity are its description. And it is not permissible to speak about it by causal reasoning. Rather, we say: The absolute (itlaq) of his saying 'No marriage except with witnesses' indicates the non-requirement of uprightness and masculinity. And al-Shafi'i requires them, i.e., uprightness and masculinity, due to his saying: 'No marriage except with a guardian and two upright witnesses,' and because it (marriage) is not property as we have transmitted earlier. And al-batira', diminutive of batra' which is the feminine of abtar, and it means prayer with one rak'ah. It is an example of a ruling: i.e., establishing whether this prayer is legislated or not. And it is not permissible to speak about it by opinion and cause. 'Take' O Muhammad, 'from their wealth' (At-Tawbah: 103) meaning those who lagged behind from jihad like Abu Lubaba who came with remorse and repentance, 'a charity to purify them' (At-Tawbah: 103) O Muhammad, by the charity, 'and cleanse them thereby' (At-Tawbah: 103) i.e., by the charity. (Al-Qamar) And 'because it is not property' etc., i.e., because marriage is not property, it resembles hadd punishments and qisas, and the testimony of women in these is not accepted, so likewise it is not permissible in marriage, so masculinity is required in witnesses of marriage. (Al-Sunbuli) We transmitted earlier: i.e., regarding the mention of corrupt causal explanations. (Al-Qamar) Al-abtar originally means one with a cut tail, then it was used to refer to one who is deficient. (Al-Qamar) Al-Bayhaqi narrated it, and al-Zayla'i said it is strange. And there is a hadith in its meaning from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet said: 'The prostitutes who marry themselves without evidence.' Al-Tirmidhi and others narrated it. He said: The correct version is from Ibn 'Abbas as a mawquf: 'No marriage except with evidence.' And 'Abd al-Razzaq narrated it as mawquf on him. And there will come to you more detail on this. [Ishraq al-Absar: 30] ** Al-Tirmidhi narrated no. 1089, chapter on announcement of marriage, from 'A'ishah who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Announce this marriage, and hold it in the mosques, and beat the duffs for it.' Al-Tirmidhi said: This hadith is gharib hasan in this chapter. Al-Daraqutni narrated it from 'A'ishah, and in it is Yazid ibn Sinan and his father. Al-Daraqutni said: He and his father are weak. Al-Nasa'i said: He is abandoned in hadith. Ahmad and others weakened him. [Ishraq al-Absar: 30]
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh explanation of the categories of what is established by ta'lil (causal reasoning). The author begins by listing three examples of things that are NOT established by ra'y or 'illah but rather by explicit nass (text). First, the condition (shart) of witnesses in marriage: the Hanafi position requires witnesses based on the explicit hadith 'No marriage except with witnesses.' Contrary to Malik who relies on the hadith about announcing marriage. Second, the description of a condition (wasf al-shart) like the requirement of uprightness ('adalah) and masculinity (dhukurah) in witnesses: Hanafis hold that the absolute command in the hadith does not stipulate these qualities, so they are not required. Shafi'i requires them based on another hadith and because marriage is not property, analogizing to hadd punishments where women's testimony is not accepted. Third, the ruling (hukm) of al-batira' (prayer with one rak'ah): the Hanafi position is that it is prohibited because the Prophet forbade it. These three categories demonstrate that certain legal elements are fixed by text and cannot be altered by qiyas. The passage then provides extensive hadith references and commentary from later scholars like al-Qamar and al-Sunbuli, reinforcing the Hanafi methodology of prioritizing nass over ra'y in foundational legal rulings.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that is essential for the validity of an act, established by nass (text) in this context.
وصف الشرط (wasf al-shart): A description or attribute of a condition, e.g., 'adalah and dhukurah as descriptions of witnesses.
حكم (hukm): A ruling or legal status, e.g., whether al-batira' prayer is legislated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, are the conditions of witnesses in marriage (like 'adalah and dhukurah) established by qiyas or by nass?
A) By qiyas
B) By nass
C) By istihsan
D) By ijma'

Key Takeaway
Conditions, descriptions of conditions, and rulings must be established by direct nass; qiyas cannot create or negate them.

Conditions of Ijtihad: The Three Required Knowledges
Lists and explains the conditions for a mujtahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three primary conditions for a mujtahid according to Hanafi usul and explain the rationale for the required scope of knowledge.

Core Idea
A mujtahid must know enough Quran, Sunnah, and Qiyas to derive rulings — not everything.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar lays out the essential qualifications for a mujtahid. It clarifies that complete mastery of all revealed texts is not required; only the portions relevant to legal rulings. The discussion also touches on why consensus is not listed as a separate condition and adds extra requirements like integrity and intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three required knowledges
What must the mujtahid know? The text specifies knowledge of the Quran, the Sunnah, and the 'aspects of qiyas' (analogical reasoning).
2
Determine the scope for each
How much is needed? About 500 Quranic verses, about 3000 hadith, and the methods/conditions of qiyas.
The author compiled these verses in his own work 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'.
3
Understand why consensus is omitted
Unlike Quran and Sunnah, consensus does not involve derivation (istinbat); it is a settled source. Therefore no condition of knowledge for ijtihad from consensus is needed.
4
Note the additional conditions
Beyond the three knowledges, the mujtahid needs linguistic skills (Arabic sciences), legal understanding, integrity (adalah), and sincere intention.
Some scholars also require piety and avoidance of partisanship.
5
Recognize the Hanafi specificity
This list reflects Hanafi usul; other schools may differ on scope or additional conditions.
The text follows the Hanafi tradition, citing commentaries like al-Qamar and al-Sunbali.

Source Text

باب القياس
بيان شرط الاجتهاد
وشرط الاجتهاد أن يحوي علم الكتاب بمعانيه اللغوية والشرعية ووجوهه التي قلنا من أي أقسامه الخاص والعام، والأمر والنهي، وسائر الأقسام السابقة، ولكن لا يشترط علم جميع الكتاب، بل قدر ما يتعلق به الأحكام وتستنبط هي منه، وذلك قدر خمس مائة آية التي ألّفتُها وجمعتها أنا في "التفسير الأحمدي". أي الأحكام أي علم السنة
وعلم السنة بطرقها المذكورة في أقسامها مع أقسام الكتاب وذلك أيضا قدر ما يتعلق به الأحكام أعني ثلاث آلاف دون سائرها. وأن يعرف وجوه القياس بطرقها وشرائطها المذكورة آنفا، ولم يذكر الإجماع اقتداءً بالسلف؛ ولأنه لا يتعلق به فائدة الاختلاف بالاستنباط، وإنما يحتاج إليه لأن يعلم المسائل بالإجماع أي اختلاف المجتهدين
علم الإجماع
عن
ظهر
وشرط الاجتهاد إلخ واعلم أن الاجتهاد بذل الطاقة من الفقيه في تحصيل حكم شرعي ظني، وقوله: أن يحوي علم الكتاب أي بعد صحة إيمانه فإنه شرط في كل عبادة، وأيضا الاجتهاد استخراج الحكم، فلا بد من معرفة الحاكم ومن هو وسيلة في تبليغ الأحكام وسائر صفاته (السنبلي أن يحوي إلخ: سواء كان حافظًا القلب أو لا . (القمر) اللغوية بأن يعرف معاني المفردات والمركبات وخواصها في الإفادة إما بالسليقة أو بإعانة العلوم كاللغة والصرف والنحو والمعاني والبيان. (القمر) والشرعية : بأن يعرف المعاني المؤثرة في الأحكام. (القمر) ولكن لا يشترط إلخ إلا أن الأولى أن يكون له علم القصص أيضًا فإنها يحتمل أن يستخرج منها أحكام (القمر) وعلم السنة: أي متنا، ولا بد من علم أحوال رجال الحديث ورواته حتى يميّز الصحاح عن الضعاف والغرائب (القمر) بطرقها: أي طرق السنة أسانيدها وأقسامها من المتواتر والآحاد وغيرها. (القمر) وجوه القياس: أي أقسامه حتى يميّز القياس الصحيح الواجب العمل عن الفاسد السقيم، ومن ههنا أنه يكون للمجتهد حظ وافر من علم الأصول، وأما عدالة المجتهد فيشترط لقبول قوله، فإن قبول قول الفاسق متوقف فيه، وبعضهم اشترطوا شرطًا زائدا، وهو أن يكون قصده معرفة خائفا الأحكام وتعليمها لا التعصب والشهرة والريا، والسمعة وينبغي أن يكون صاحب ورع منه تعالى وقت الاجتهاد فإنه أعين الشرع (القمر) بطرقها : أي يعلم سندها الذي رُويت به أحاد، ويعلم تواتره وشهرته مع العلم بحال الرواة، بحر العلوم (السنبلي) اقتداء بالسلف: فإنهم لا يذكرون الإجماع. (القمر) يعني
Chapter of Analogical Reasoning. Statement of the condition of ijtihad. The condition of ijtihad is that [the mujtahid] must encompass knowledge of the Book with its linguistic and legal meanings, and its aspects that we mentioned: which of its divisions are specific and general, command and prohibition, and the rest of the preceding divisions. However, knowledge of the entire Book is not required, only as much as pertains to rulings and from which they are derived, and that is about five hundred verses which I authored and collected in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'. (Meaning for rulings) meaning knowledge of the Sunnah. And knowledge of the Sunnah with its methods mentioned in its divisions along with the divisions of the Book, and that is also as much as pertains to rulings, namely three thousand [hadiths] excluding others. And that he know the aspects of analogical reasoning with its methods and conditions mentioned above. He did not mention consensus following the predecessors; because no benefit of difference through derivation attaches to it, rather one needs it to know the issues by consensus. (Meaning difference of the mujtahideen) Knowledge of consensus. And the condition of ijtihad etc. Know that ijtihad is the exertion of effort by the faqih to obtain a speculative legal ruling. His saying: 'he encompass knowledge of the Book' meaning after the soundness of his faith, for it is a condition in every act of worship. Also ijtihad is the extraction of the ruling, so it is necessary to know the Ruler and that which is a means in conveying rulings and His other attributes. (al-Sunbali: he encompass etc.: whether he memorizes by heart or not.) (al-Qamar) Linguistic [meanings]: that he know the meanings of words, compounds, and their properties in conveying meaning, either by natural disposition or with the aid of sciences such as lexicography, morphology, syntax, rhetoric, and eloquence. (al-Qamar) And legal [meanings]: that he know the meanings that affect rulings. (al-Qamar) But it is not a condition except that it is preferable that he also have knowledge of the stories, for it is possible that rulings may be derived from them. (al-Qamar) And knowledge of the Sunnah: meaning the text, and it is necessary to have knowledge of the conditions of the men of hadith and its narrators so that he can distinguish authentic from weak and anomalous ones. (al-Qamar) With its methods: meaning the methods of the Sunnah, its chains and divisions from mutawatir, ahad, and others. (al-Qamar) Aspects of qiyas: meaning its divisions so that he can distinguish sound qiyas that is obligatory to act upon from corrupt and unsound [qiyas], and from here it is that the mujtahid has a sufficient share of usul al-fiqh. As for the integrity of the mujtahid, it is a condition for acceptance of his statement, for acceptance of a wrongdoer's statement is suspended. And some added an extra condition: that his intention be to know the rulings and teach them, not partisanship, fame, hypocrisy, and reputation, and he should be a person of piety for Allah Almighty at the time of ijtihad, for it is a greater help for the law. (al-Qamar) With its methods: meaning he know the chain by which it was narrated as ahad, and he know its tawatur and fame with knowledge of the condition of the narrators (Bahr al-Ulum) (al-Sunbali) Following the predecessors: for they do not mention consensus. (al-Qamar) Meaning
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling text specifying the conditions for a mujtahid. It states three required knowledges: Quran, Sunnah, and Qiyas. For the Quran, the mujtahid must know its linguistic and legal meanings and its divisions (like general/specific, command/prohibition), but only about five hundred verses that pertain to rulings—the author has compiled these in his 'Tafsir Ahmadi'. For the Sunnah, about three thousand hadiths are needed, along with knowledge of their chains and classifications (mutawatir, ahad). For Qiyas, the mujtahid must know its methods and conditions to distinguish valid from invalid analogy. Consensus is not mentioned as a condition because, unlike Quran and Sunnah, consensus does not allow for different derivations; it is a settled matter. The commentary elaborates: ijtihad is exertion to obtain a speculative ruling; faith is prerequisite; the mujtahid must know Arabic sciences for linguistic meanings and legal theory for legal meanings. He must also examine hadith narrators to authenticate traditions. Extra conditions include uprightness (adalah) and sincere intention. The passage follows the Hanafi tradition, and the authority is from the commentaries on Noor al-Anwar.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Exertion of effort by the faqih to obtain a speculative (zanni) legal ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; applying a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to shared illah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Approximately how many Quranic verses does the text state are needed for ijtihad?
A) 500
B) 3000
C) All of the Quran
D) 100

Key Takeaway
The mujtahid's knowledge must be sufficient for derivation — not exhaustive, but adequate in Quran, Sunnah, and Qiyas.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Two Types of Rulings Established by Proofs
Defining the two-fold outcome of proofs (taklifi and wad'i rulings) and clarifying qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between taklifi and wad'i rulings and explain why qiyas does not establish new rulings.

Core Idea
Proofs establish two things: taklifi (duty) and wad'i (conditional) rulings; qiyas only extends.

This passage transitions from studying the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas) to examining the rulings they produce. It introduces the foundational taxonomy of Islamic rulings—taklifi and wad'i—and clarifies the unique role of qiyas. Mastering this taxonomy is essential for all further fiqh and usul analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories established by proofs
Read the passage and extract the two things that the proofs (except qiyas) establish. Note their Arabic names.
The categories are al-ahkam al-taklifiyya and al-ahkam al-wad'iyya.
2
Understand the exclusion of qiyas
Find the reason the author excludes qiyas from the list of proofs that 'establish.' Recognize that qiyas does not create a new ruling but extends an existing one.
Qiyas is for extension (ta'diya), not establishment (ithbat).
3
Differentiate taklifi and wad'i rulings
Define each category: taklifi rulings impose a duty or prohibition on the mukallaf; wad'i rulings set causes, conditions, or preventers.
Wad'i rulings include sabab, shart, and mani'.
4
Examine the definition of hukm from al-Tawdih
Note the three elements of a ruling: al-Hakim (Allah), al-mahkum 'alayh (the mukallaf), and al-mahkum bih (the action). Rulings are qualities of the action like obligation, prohibition, etc.
The passage uses this to ground the discussion.

Then when the author finished discussing the four proofs, he wanted to discuss after that what is established by the proofs. And I have said previously: Indeed the subject of usul al-fiqh according to the chosen madhhab is the proofs and the rulings all together. Then he said: ... [marginal notes] ... Section on Rulings. Prelude to the divisions of rulings. If one intends the general meaning of establishedness, it is possible that by 'proofs' are meant the four proofs, and by 'rulings' are meant the taklifi rulings, and by 'what relates to the rulings' are meant the wad'i rulings. They have mentioned these rules scattered. And what is known from al-Tawdih in their classification is that the ruling depends on the Ruler (Allah), the subject (the mukallaf), and the object (the action of the mukallaf including acts of worship, punishments, etc.). The rulings are qualities of the action of the mukallaf such as obligation, prohibition, etc. [marginal notes continue]
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage marks the transition from discussing the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas) to discussing the rulings (ahkam) themselves. The author first clarifies the subject of usul al-fiqh: it covers both proofs and rulings. He then categorizes what the proofs establish into two categories: (1) the taklifi rulings (obligation, prohibition, etc.) and (2) the wad'i rulings (cause, condition, preventer). There is a subtle point about qiyas: unlike the other three proofs, qiyas does not directly 'establish' a new ruling; it merely extends an existing ruling from an original case to a branch case. Thus, qiyas is excluded from the immediate discussion of 'what is established by proofs.' The passage also gives a brief definition of a ruling (hukm) from al-Tawdih: it has three elements — the Lawgiver (Allah), the subject (the mukallaf), and the object (the action). The rulings themselves are the qualities attributed to the action, like obligation, etc. The marginal notes further clarify that wad'i rulings include causality, conditionality, and preventiveness.

Key Terms
الأحكام التكليفية (al-aḥkām al-taklīfiyya): Rulings that impose a duty or prohibition on the mukallaf, such as wajib, haram, mandub, etc.
الأحكام الوضعية (al-aḥkām al-waḍ‘iyya): Rulings that set conditions, causes, or preventers for taklifi rulings, e.g., sabab, shart, mani'.
الحكم (al-ḥukm): A quality ascribed to the action of the mukallaf by the Shari'ah address, e.g., wajib, haram, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The proofs of Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma' directly establish taklifi and wad'i rulings; qiyas only extends existing rulings.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

The Four Categories of Rulings: Rights of Allah and Rights of Servants
Explaining the division of rulings into four categories based on rights of Allah and servants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given ruling into one of the four categories based on the dominant right, and explain the Hanafi position on the qadhf punishment dispute.

Core Idea
Rulings are categorized by whose right is dominant: Allah's or the servant's, determining pardon and inheritance.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the nature of a ruling—whether it is a right of Allah (haqq Allah) or a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd)—is crucial for determining its legal effects, such as whether it can be pardoned or inherited. This passage introduces a four-fold classification that refines the basic dichotomy by recognizing mixed categories where one right dominates. Familiarity with this taxonomy is essential for correctly applying rulings on hudud, qisas, and property.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the concept of haqq in usul al-fiqh
Distinguish between rights of Allah (general benefit, not subject to waiver) and rights of servants (specific personal benefit, subject to waiver). Understand that these are analytical categories, not actual claims.
The text emphasizes that Allah does not benefit; the classification is based on the purpose of the rule.
2
Identify the four categories
From the passage, list the categories: (1) pure haqq Allah, (2) pure haqq al-'abd, (3) mixed with haqq Allah dominant, (4) mixed with haqq al-'abd dominant. Note that no category has equal rights on both sides.
The author explicitly states there is no fifth category where both are equal.
3
Apply the categories to given examples
Match each example from the passage to its category: zina → pure haqq Allah; property inviolability → pure haqq al-'abd; hadd al-qadhf → mixed with haqq Allah dominant (Hanafi view); qisas → mixed with haqq al-'abd dominant.
Consider why hadd al-qadhf is not purely haqq Allah: it also involves the victim's honor.
4
Analyze the dispute on qadhf
Examine the Shafi'i position that haqq al-'abd is dominant in hadd al-qadhf, leading to different legal consequences (inheritance, pardon possible). Trace the Hanafi argument: the prohibition of zina is pure haqq Allah, so the punishment for falsely attributing zina is likewise primarily Allah's right.
The thamarah (practical result) is that in Hanafi, the victim cannot pardon the hadd.
5
Recognize the legal effects (thamarah)
For each category, infer the consequences: pure haqq Allah → no waiver or inheritance; pure haqq al-'abd → can be waived by the individual; mixed with haqq Allah dominant → generally no waiver/inheritance; mixed with haqq al-'abd dominant → can be waived/inherited (e.g., qisas).
This step links the classification to practical fiqh rulings.

بيان أقسام الأحكام إلى الله تعالى تعظيما، وإلا فالله تعالى عن أن ينتفع بشيء، فلا يجوز أن يكون حقا له بهذا الوجه ولا بجهة التخليق؛ لأن الكل سواء في ذلك.

والرابع ما اجتمعا فيه وحق العبد غالب كالقصاص، فإن فيه حق الله، وهو إخلاء العالم عن الفساد، وحق العبد لوقوع الجناية على نفسه، سواء في ذلك فإنه تعالى خالق كل شيء. كحرمة مال الغير فإنها حق العبد لتعلّق صيانة مال العبد بها.
(القمر) ولهذا: أي لكونه مصلحة خاصة.
المحشي) يباح أي مال الغير بإباحة المالك، ولا يباح الزنا بإباحة أهل المزنية.
(القمر) ما اجتمعا أي حق الله تعالى وحق العبد
(القمر) كحد القذف أي جلد القاذف ثمانين جلدة، وعدم قبول شهادته أبدا، وإنما وجب هذا الحد للانزجار والاجتناب عن فاحشة كبيرة.
(القمر) من حيث أنه جزاء هتك إلخ فيفيد نفع عام أي صون العالم عن الفساد
(القمر) غالب إلخ: فإن سبب وجوب هذا الحد هتك عرض المقذوف وعرضه حقه ونحن نقول: إن حد القذف إنما يجب إذا قذف محصنًا بالزنا، وحرمة الزنا خالصة لله تعالى، فكما أن حدّ الزنا خالص حقه تعالى كذلك حد إظهار الزنا خالص حقه تعالى، إلا أن القاذف هتك حرمة المقذوف، وللمقذوف حق في عرضه كما أن الله تعالى أيضا حقا في عرضه، فثبت أن للعبد فيه ضرب حق، والحق الغالب الله تعالى.
(القمر) الإرث : بأن مات المقذوف ويدعي ورثته فليس لهم إجراء الحد؛ لأن الإرث خلافة ، والخلافة لا تجري في حق الله تعالى.
(القمر) والعفو أي لا يجري فيه العفو، فلا يسقط بعفو المقذوف، إلا في رواية بشر عن أبي يوسف الله، فإن العبد إنما يُسقط ما يكون حقا أو كان فيه حقه غالبًا، وما ليس كذلك فلا يملك إسقاطه.
(القمر) فتنعكس إلخ: أي يجري فيه الإرث والعفو
(القمر) ما اجتمعا أي حق الله تعالى وحق العبد، ولم يوجد قسم خامس، أي ما اجتمع فيه حق العبد والله على التساوي.
(القمر) على نفسه: أي على نفس العبد، ففي القصاص جبر انكسار قلب ورثة المقتول. (القمر)
Section on Rulings. Explanation of the divisions of rulings. The first division is the rights of Allah exclusively, and they are [rulings] related to a general benefit, such as the prohibition of zinā and the like, and this is with respect to the Exaltedness of Allah; otherwise, Allah is above benefiting from anything, so it is not permissible for it to be a right of His in this respect nor in respect of creation, because all are equal in that. [This is] in the sense of benefit. The second is the rights of servants exclusively, which is that which pertains to a specific benefit, such as the inviolability of another's property, and for this reason it becomes permissible with the permission of the owner. [Meaning] worldly. In theft and usurpation. The third is that in which both [rights] are combined and the right of Allah is dominant, such as the prescribed punishment for qadhf, for in it is the right of Allah in that it is a punishment for violating the honor of a chaste and righteous person, and the right of the servant in that it removes the disgrace of the accused, but the right of Allah is dominant to the extent that inheritance and pardon do not apply to it. According to al-Shāfiʿī, the right of the servant is dominant in it, so the rulings are reversed. The fourth is that in which both are combined and the right of the servant is dominant, such as qiṣāṣ, for in it is the right of Allah, which is clearing the world of corruption, and the right of the servant due to the transgression being upon his own self. All are equal in that, for He is the Creator of everything. Like the inviolability of another's property, it is the right of the servant due to the protection of the servant's property. (al-Qamar) And for this reason: i.e., because it is a specific benefit. (al-Muḥashshī) The property of another is permitted by the permission of the owner, but zinā is not permitted by the permission of the woman. (al-Qamar) In which both are combined, i.e., the right of Allah and the right of the servant. (al-Qamar) Like the hadd of qadhf, i.e., flogging the accuser eighty lashes and the permanent rejection of his testimony, and this punishment was obligated for deterrence and avoidance of a major obscenity. (al-Qamar) In that it is a punishment for violating... etc., it provides a general benefit, i.e., preserving the world from corruption. (al-Qamar) dominates etc.: for the cause of this punishment is the violation of the honor of the accused, and his honor is his right. And we say: The hadd of qadhf is obligated only if one accuses a chaste person of zinā, and the prohibition of zinā is exclusively the right of Allah. Just as the hadd of zinā is exclusively His right, so also the hadd of manifesting zinā is exclusively His right. However, the accuser violated the honor of the accused, and the accused has a right in his honor just as Allah also has a right in his honor. So it is established that the servant has some right in it, but the dominant right is Allah's. (al-Qamar) Inheritance: i.e., if the accused dies and his heirs claim, they do not have the right to execute the hadd, because inheritance is succession, and succession does not apply to the right of Allah. (al-Qamar) Pardon: i.e., pardon does not apply to it, so it is not dropped by the pardon of the accused, except in the narration of Bishr from Abū Yūsuf, for the servant only drops what is his right or in which his right is dominant, and what is not like that, he cannot drop it. (al-Qamar) They are reversed, etc.: i.e., inheritance and pardon apply in it. (al-Qamar) Both are combined, i.e., the right of Allah and the right of the servant, and there is no fifth division, i.e., where both rights are equal. (al-Qamar) Upon his own self: i.e., upon the servant's self, for in qiṣāṣ there is reparation for the broken heart of the heirs of the slain. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a chapter on rulings, where the author explains the division of shar'i rulings into four categories based on whose right is predominant. The first category is rulings that are purely the right of Allah, such as the prohibition of zina. These are established for the general benefit, not because Allah benefits from them. The second category is rulings that are purely the right of servants, such as the inviolability of property; the owner's permission makes it permissible. The third category is mixed, with Allah's right dominant, as in the hadd punishment for false accusation of zina (qadhf): Allah's right to uphold morality is dominant, and thus the punishment cannot be inherited or pardoned by the victim. The Shafi'i school differs, holding that the servant's right is dominant here, allowing pardon and inheritance. The fourth category is mixed with the servant's right dominant, as in qisas: the victim's heirs have the right to demand retaliation, and they can pardon or accept compensation, showing the precedence of the servant's right. The commentary further explains each category with examples and clarifies that no category has equal rights on both sides. This classification is fundamental in Hanafi usul for understanding the nature of hukm and the effects of inheritance, pardon, and other legal consequences.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): A ruling that primarily serves to uphold the order of society and obedience to Allah, not a specific individual's benefit. Examples include hudud punishments (except those with a servant's right), acts of worship.
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): A ruling that primarily pertains to a specific individual's personal benefit, such as property rights or personal retaliation, and can be waived by that individual.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The prescribed punishment for falsely accusing a chaste person of zina: eighty lashes and permanent rejection of testimony. It is a mixed category with Allah's right dominant in Hanafi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which category does the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of zina) belong to?
A) Pure right of Allah
B) Pure right of the servant
C) Mixed with Allah's right dominant
D) Mixed with the servant's right dominant

Key Takeaway
The classification of rulings into four categories based on the dominant right determines key legal consequences such as pardon and inheritance, and the dispute on qadhf illustrates how different ijtihads lead to different practical rulings.

The Classification of Iman and Its Branches in Hanafi Usul
Definition and classification of the first type of Allah's rights: pure worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the Hanafi tripartite division of faith (iman) into origin (asl), appendage (lahiq), and additions (zawa'id), and state the relationship between iman and its branches.

Core Idea
Iman is rooted in internal attestation; acts of worship are its branches, valid only when built upon that foundation.

This passage introduces the first of eight categories of Allah's rights: pure acts of worship. The author states that iman and its branches (prayer, zakat, fasting, hajj) belong to this category. The commentary then subcategorizes these acts into three types: origins, appendages, and additions. This classification is essential for understanding how Hanafi usul relates belief to action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Claim
Read the passage and note that the rights of Allah are eight types. The first type is pure worship, including iman and its branches. Understand that iman is the foundation and the acts are branches because they are invalid without iman, but iman is valid without them.
2
Recognize the Commentary's Expansion
The commentary explains that ‘iman’ (in the context of the eight types) refers to the belief, and the acts of worship are three types: origins (usul), appendages (lawahiq), and additions (zawa'id). This tripartite division applies to the totality of iman and its branches, not to each individually.
This is a key principle: the classification is holistic.
3
Trace the Logical Relationship
Understand why acts are branches of iman: they are not valid without iman, but iman is valid without performing the acts. This establishes the priority of internal faith over external actions.
This underpins the Hanafi doctrine that faith is primarily internal.
4
Apply the Tripartite Division
For iman: origin (asl) is internal attestation (tasdiq), appendage (lahiq) is verbal declaration (iqrar), and additions (zawa'id) are the remaining branches such as prayer, zakat, etc. Alternatively, another view holds that additions in iman itself are the repetition of the shahada.
The alternative view shows the flexibility in Hanafi usul.
5
Note the Hierarchy Among Branches
Among the branches, prayer is the origin because it is the pillar of religion. Zakat is appended to prayer, and other acts are similarly appended. This hierarchy affects prioritization in legal rulings.
This hierarchy is not explicit in the base but derived from the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام حقوق الله
بالاستقراء
وحقوق الله ثمانية أنواع: عبادات، خالصة، لا يَشُوبُها معنى العقوبة والمؤنة كالإيمان وفروعه، وهي الصلاة والزكاة والصوم والحج، وإنما كانت فروعًا للإيمان؛ لأنها لا تصح بدونه، وهو صحيح بدونها.
أي الإيمان وهي أي العبادات أنواع ثلاثة أصول ولواحق وزوائد يعني إن في مجموع الإيمان وفروعه هذه الثلاثة، لا أن في كل منهما هذه الثلاثة، فالإيمان أصله التصديق، والملحق به الإقرار، والزوائد هي الفروع الباقية، أو نقول : الزوائد في الإيمان هي تكرار الشهادة، والأصل في الفروع الصلاة؛ لأنها عماد الدين، ثم الزكاة ملحقة بها؛ كالصلاة وغيرها
Explanation of the divisions of the rights of Allah. By induction. The rights of Allah are eight types: acts of worship that are pure, not mixed with the meaning of punishment or burden, such as Īmān and its branches, and they are prayer, zakāt, fasting, and Ḥajj. They are branches of Īmān because they are not valid without it, while it is valid without them. [Commentary:] i.e., Īmān. And these acts of worship are three types: origins (uṣūl), appendages (lawāḥiq), and additions (zawāʾid). That is, within the totality of Īmān and its branches there are these three, not that each of them has these three. For Īmān, its origin is attestation (taṣdīq), and attached to it is declaration (iqrār), and the additions are the remaining branches. Or we say: the additions in Īmān are the repetition of the testimony. The origin among the branches is prayer, because it is the pillar of religion; then zakāt is appended to it, like prayer and others.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage begins the explanation of the divisions of the rights of Allah, which the author states are eight types based on inductive reasoning. Only the first type is discussed here: pure acts of worship that are not mixed with any aspect of punishment or burden. These include Iman (faith) and its branches: prayer, zakat, fasting, and hajj. The author then explains that these acts of worship (the branches) are considered branches of Iman because they are not valid without Iman, but Iman is valid without them (i.e., one can be a believer without performing the physical acts). The commentary further subcategorizes Iman and its branches into three: the origin (asl) which is the attestation of the heart (tasdiq), the appendage (lahiq) which is the verbal declaration (iqrar), and the additions (zawa'id) which are the remaining branches such as prayer, zakat, etc. Another view is that the additions in Iman are the repetition of the shahada. Among the branches, the origin is prayer, as it is the pillar of religion, and then zakat is appended to prayer. This classification helps in understanding the hierarchy of faith and its manifestations in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
عبادات خالصة ('ibādāt khāliṣah): Pure acts of worship that are free from any element of punishment or burden; they are solely for obedience and closeness to Allah. Examples: Iman, prayer, zakat, fasting, hajj.
الإيمان (al-īmān): The origin (asl) of faith is the attestation of the heart (tasdiq), and its appendage is the verbal declaration. It is the foundation upon which branches like prayer are built.
أصول ولواحق وزوائد (uṣūl wa lawāḥiq wa zawāʾid): Three categories of the totality of Iman and its branches: origins (asl like tasdiq), appendages (lahiq like iqrar), and additions (zawa'id like prayer, zakat, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The declaration (shahada)
B) The prayer (salah)
C) The attestation of the heart (tasdiq)
D) The charity (zakat)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of faith into origin, appendage, and additions clarifies that acts of worship are branches dependent on iman, not constitutive of it.

The Hanafi Classification of Rights of Allah: 'Ibadat and 'Uqubat
Expansion defining the three categories of Rights of Allah and their subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three main categories of rights of Allah in Hanafi usul, distinguish between complete and incomplete punishments, and explain the hierarchy among the acts of worship.

Core Idea
Rights of Allah are of three types: pure worship, complete punishments, and incomplete punishments — each with distinct legal characteristics.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi classification of the rights of Allah (huquq Allah). Through the lens of usul al-fiqh, we examine the eight types, focusing on the three primary categories of pure worship (ibadat), complete punishments (uqubat kamilah), and incomplete punishments (uqubat qasirah). Understanding these categories is essential for knowing how rulings are derived and applied in different contexts, such as the requirement of intention in worship or the applicability of a punishment to a minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
From the passage, list the three categories of rights of Allah: pure acts of worship, complete punishments, and incomplete punishments. Notice that the author also mentions eight types but focuses on these three.
The eight types are subsumed under these three.
2
Examine the hierarchy of acts of worship
Trace the ranking from faith (iman) through prayer, zakat, fasting, pilgrimage, to jihad. Note the reasons given: bodily vs. wealth, personal vs. communal obligation.
Pay attention to the commentary's explanation of why each is lower than the previous.
3
Distinguish complete and incomplete punishments
Identify the examples of complete punishments (hudud: lashing, stoning) and incomplete (deprivation of inheritance). Note the criterion: presence or absence of physical pain or financial loss to the perpetrator.
This distinction affects who can be punished (e.g., minors can be subject to incomplete punishments).
4
Apply the classification to derive rulings
Use the definitions to derive consequences: e.g., pure worship requires intention; incomplete punishments can apply to minors.
This shows the practical import of the classification.

Chapter on Rulings: Statement of the Categories of the Rights of Allah. [It is predominant for the flow of inheritance and the validity of exchanging it for property by compromise and the validity of forgiveness. Statement of the categories of the rights of Allah] by induction. And the rights of Allah are eight kinds: Pure acts of worship, which are not mixed with the meaning of punishment and expense, like faith and its branches, and they are prayer, zakat, fasting, and pilgrimage. They are only branches of faith because they are not valid without it, and it is valid without them. That is, faith, and they (the acts of worship) are of three types: foundations, attachments, and additions. That is, in the entirety of faith and its branches are these three, not that each of them is divided into these three. So faith, its foundation is belief, and what is attached to it is declaration, and the additions are the remaining branches. Or we say: the additions in faith are the repetition of the testimony. And the foundation among the branches is prayer, because it is the pillar of religion, then zakat is attached to it; like prayer and others. The flow of inheritance: the heirs of the slain own retaliation. (Al-Qamar) And the validity of exchange etc.: if the heirs of the slain accept property in exchange for retaliation by compromise, it is permissible. (Al-Qamar) And the validity of forgiveness: the forgiveness of the heirs of the slain for the crime of the killer is valid, so he is not punished with retaliation by the Lawgiver. (Al-Qamar) Like faith, etc., and it is the foundation of acts of worship, because no act of worship is valid without it. And his saying: "And they are prayer," I say: It is the foundation of acts of worship after faith, because it is the pillar of religion. And his saying: "And zakat," I say: It is that which is connected to the blessing of wealth, which is below the soul. (Al-Sunbali) Not valid without it: faith is a condition for the validity of all actions, for if one does not believe in Allah Almighty, how can one draw near to Him through worship? (Al-Qamar) Without it: so it is not objected that jihad is excluded from it, because it is not a foundation. (Al-Muhashi) The acts of worship: that is, the entirety of faith and its branches, like prayer and others. (Al-Muhashi) The totality of faith, etc.: the entirety of faith and its branches is divided into these three types, not that each of them is divided into these three types. (Al-Qamar) Its foundation is belief: that is, by the heart, for it is a firm foundation that does not admit of falling. (Al-Qamar) Declaration: declaration is a translation of what is in the conscience, and the mine of belief is the heart, so it became attached to faith, and thus it may be excused due to coercion and dumbness. (Al-Qamar) Prayer, etc.: because it is the pillar of religion, no law of the messengers is empty of it, and it includes service with the apparent body like standing and others, and with the inner like intention and humility and others. But since it became a means of proximity through the House [Ka'ba], it was below faith. Then zakat, which is wealth, and it is below prayer because the blessing of the body is fundamental and the blessing of wealth is subsidiary. Then fasting, which is connected to the blessing of the body, and it is a means of proximity attached to prayer; fasting is discipline and prayer is service and intimate conversation with the Lord. And because it is for the blessing of the body: wealth is protection of the soul, so what is attached to the subsidiary (zakat) is dependent and inferior, and what is attached to the fundamental (prayer) is fundamental. (Al-Qamar) For subduing the soul: i.e., the soul that commands evil, so fasting is only prescribed by means of the evil soul, and this means is lower than the means in zakat, because the soul here is not external to the worshipper, unlike the means in zakat, which is external to him. Ibn al-Malik said: The soul inclines to desires, which is an ugly quality in it, and there is no ugliness in the quality of poverty, so it was stronger as a means. (Al-Qamar) Then pilgrimage: it is like a means to fasting, so it became lower than it, because the pilgrim intends pilgrimage, abandons homelands, family, and children, and cuts off from the sources of desires in the deserts; his soul weakens, its satanic nature leaves, and he is able to subdue it through fasting. (Al-Qamar) Then jihad: it was only prescribed to remove the disbelief of the disbeliever, otherwise it is inherently ugly because it is destruction of the lands of Allah and torment of the servants of Allah. Then it is a communal obligation, and the previous acts of worship are personal obligations, so it became lower than what preceded. (Al-Qamar) And at that time: i.e., when the foundations and attachments are realized in these branches, the additions: i.e., supererogatory acts of worship, i.e., supererogatory fasting, prayer, zakat, and pilgrimage. (Al-Qamar) And complete punishments: i.e., perfect ones. They are only called punishments because they follow the sin and are retribution for it. (Al-Qamar) In that they are, etc., relates to the author's statement analyzing "complete," and this indicates that the legislation of punishments like hudud is for deterrence and prevention from committing sins, and the otherworldly punishment is not remitted by them, contemplate. (Al-Qamar) The punishment for adultery: i.e., one hundred lashes for the non-muhsan and stoning for the muhsan. (Al-Qamar) And the punishment for drinking: i.e., drinking wine, which is eighty lashes, and likewise the punishment for slander. (Al-Qamar) Deprivation of inheritance: i.e., depriving the killer of inheritance. (Al-Qamar) And this: i.e., deprivation of inheritance is incomplete compared to it, for there is no pain in deprivation of inheritance on the apparent body, and no diminution in the wealth of that heir. (Al-Qamar) And for this reason: i.e., because deprivation of inheritance is an incomplete punishment, not a complete one, a minor is subject to it, for if he kills his testator intentionally or by mistake, he is deprived of inheritance. And in it there is opposition to what is in "Al-Tahqiq" where it said: since it is an incomplete punishment, it is not established in the case of a minor, so even if he kills...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work explaining the categories of the rights of Allah (huquq Allah). The author begins by stating that the rights of Allah are eight types, but then focuses on three major categories: pure acts of worship (ibadat), complete punishments ('uqubat kamilah), and incomplete punishments ('uqubat qasirah). The acts of worship are those with no element of punishment or expense, such as faith and its branches: prayer, zakat, fasting, and pilgrimage. The commentator elaborates that these branches have a hierarchy: the foundation is faith (tasdiq bil-qalb), followed by declaration (iqrar) as an attachment, and then the remaining branches are additions (zawa'id). Among the acts of worship, prayer is the pillar of religion, then zakat (attached to wealth), then fasting (subduing the soul), then pilgrimage (a means to fasting), and finally jihad (a communal obligation). The passage then turns to punishments. Complete punishments are the prescribed hudud, such as lashing for adultery (100 lashes for non-muhsin, stoning for muhsin), 80 lashes for drinking, and similar for slander. Incomplete punishments include deprivation of inheritance for a killer, which has no physical pain and thus can apply to minors. The text provides reasons for the hierarchy and discusses the underlying wisdom, such as the outward-inward nature of worship and the deterrent purpose of punishments.

Key Terms
حقوق الله (huqūq Allāh): The legal obligations and prohibitions that primarily pertain to divine commands, as opposed to individual rights.
عبادات (ʿibādāt): Pure acts of worship that contain no element of punishment or expense, like faith, prayer, zakat, fasting, and pilgrimage.
عقوبات كاملة (ʿuqūbāt kāmilah): Punishments that involve physical pain or material loss, such as hudud penalties, intended as comprehensive deterrence.
عقوبات قاصرة (ʿuqūbāt qāṣirah): Punishments that do not entail physical pain or financial loss to the perpetrator, like deprivation of inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, which of the following is considered an incomplete punishment (عقوبة قاصرة)?
A) Lashing for adultery
B) Deprivation of inheritance for a killer
C) Stoning for adultery
D) Eighty lashes for drinking wine

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of rights of Allah into pure worship, complete punishments, and incomplete punishments provides a framework for understanding the legal characteristics and hierarchies of divine commands.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Classification of Acts of Worship and Punishments in Hanafi Usul
Exposition of the hierarchy of acts of worship and types of punishments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi hierarchical classification of acts of worship and punishments, and apply the distinction between complete and incomplete punishments to determine legal capacity requirements.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, acts of worship are ranked by their connection to body or wealth; punishments are divided into complete (bodily) and incomplete (non-bodily), with differing capacity requirements.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar presents a systematic classification of the rights of Allah (huquq Allah). It first ranks the five pillars of worship in order of precedence, then divides punishments into complete (hudud) and incomplete (such as deprivation of inheritance). The key legal consequence is that incomplete punishments can be applied even to minors, because they do not require full legal capacity. This classification is a foundational usul tool for understanding how different obligations and penalties interact with legal personality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy of worship
List the five acts of worship in the order given in the text and note the reasoning for their ranking.
Prayer is primary (asl); zakat involves wealth (branch); fasting subdues the soul; Hajj involves hardship; Jihad is a collective duty.
2
Understand the basis for ranking
The ranking is based on the connection to the self (body) versus external wealth, and the nature of the act (personal vs communal, hardship etc.).
Zakat is strong because wealth is a branch of the body, but fasting is lower because it deals with the soul which is internal; Jihad is lowest because it is fard kifayah and involves destruction.
3
Distinguish complete and incomplete punishments
Define each: complete punishments involve bodily pain (hudud); incomplete punishments do not (e.g., deprivation of inheritance).
Complete punishments require the offender to be mukallaf (adult, sane); incomplete punishments do not require full legal capacity.
4
Apply the rule to the minor's case
Apply the distinction to the dispute: a minor who kills his testator is deprived of inheritance because it is an incomplete punishment.
This is the mufta bihi according to al-Hidayah, contrary to al-Tahqiq and al-Shafi'i.
5
Note the legal consequence
The classification directly affects which punishments can be applied to minors and other non-mukallaf individuals.
Complete punishments are only for mukallaf; incomplete ones (like loss of inheritance) can apply to minors.

فصل في الأحكام بيان أقسام حقوق الله لأن نعمة المال فرع لنعمة البدن، ثم الصوم؛ لأنه شرع لقهر النفس، ثم الحج، ثم الجهاد

القذف، وحد السرقة. أي قطع اليد وعقوبات قاصرة مثل حرمان الميراث بسبب قتل ،المورث، فإن العقوبة الكاملة القصاص في حقه، وهذا قاصر منه، ولهذا يُجزى به الصبي.

(السنبلي) لنعمة البدن: فإن المال وقاية النفس، فما تعلّق بالفرع أي الزكاة كان تابعًا ولاحقا وما تعلّق بالأصل أي الصلاة كان أصلاً. (القمر) وحينئذ أي حين تحقق الأصول واللواحق في هذه الفروع الزوائد، أي على الفرائض والواجبات هي نوافل العبادات، أي الصوم والصلاة والزكاة والحج. جلدة،

(القمر) ولهذا: أي لكون حرمان الميراث عقوبة قاصرة لا كاملة يُجزى به الصبي، فإنه إذا قتل مورثه عمدًا أو خطأ يحرم عن الميراث وفيه أنه مخالف لما في "التحقيق" حيث قال ولكونه عقوبة قاصرة لا يثبت في حق الصبي حتى لو قتل =

Chapter on rulings: Explanation of the divisions of the rights of Allah. Because the blessing of wealth is a branch of the blessing of the body, then fasting because it was legislated to subdue the soul, then Hajj, then Jihad. These branches among themselves are roots and appendages, and then the additions are the supererogatory acts of worship and their sunan. And complete punishments in that they are deterrent, like the hadd punishments: the hadd of adultery, the hadd of drinking, the hadd of slander, and the hadd of theft (i.e., amputation of the hand). And incomplete punishments like deprivation of inheritance due to killing the deceased, for the complete punishment is retribution in his case, and this is less than it, and therefore a minor is punished with it. (Commentary) ... (al-Qamar) And for this: because deprivation of inheritance is an incomplete punishment, not a complete one, it is inflicted on a minor, for if he kills his testator intentionally or unintentionally, he is deprived of inheritance, and in it there is opposition to what is in al-Tahqiq where it said that because it is an incomplete punishment it is not established against a minor, such that even if he kills his testator intentionally or unintentionally, he is not deprived of inheritance according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And it is said in al-Hidayah: Deprivation of inheritance is a punishment, and a minor is not subject to punishment. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the hierarchical classification of acts of worship and punishments in Hanafi law. It begins by stating that wealth is secondary to bodily health, and then ranks acts of worship: prayer is primary, zakat is strong because it involves wealth (a branch), fasting is for subduing the soul, Hajj involves hardships, and Jihad is the lowest because it is a collective obligation and involves destruction. The text then explains that supererogatory acts (nawafil) are additions to the obligatory. Next, punishments are divided into 'complete' (kamilah) and 'incomplete' (qasirah). Complete punishments are hudud such as lashing for adultery (100 lashes for non-muhsan, stoning for muhsan), drinking (80 lashes), slander (80 lashes), and amputation for theft. Incomplete punishments include deprivation of inheritance for killing the deceased, because it does not cause bodily pain. The text then discusses a key issue: a minor who kills his testator is deprived of inheritance according to the Hanafi school, even though al-Tahqiq suggests otherwise. The relied-upon position is that deprivation of inheritance is a punishment and a minor is not subject to punishment, so he is not deprived, contrary to al-Shafi'i. This is supported by al-Hidayah. The commentators (al-Qamar, al-Sunbali) provide detailed reasoning for the hierarchy and the nature of punishments.

Key Terms
عقوبة كاملة (uqūba kāmila): A punishment that involves bodily pain and is a prescribed hadd penalty.
عقوبة قاصرة (uqūba qāṣira): A punishment that does not involve bodily pain, such as deprivation of inheritance.
صبي (ṣabī): A minor who has not reached puberty.

A) Because killing is a major sin and minors are accountable.
B) Because deprivation of inheritance is an incomplete punishment that does not require full legal capacity.
C) Because the minor is considered an adult in property matters.
D) Because the Hanafi school follows al-Shafi'i on this issue.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of punishments into complete (bodily) and incomplete (non-bodily) determines legal capacity requirements: incomplete punishments may be applied to minors.

Categories Between Worship and Punishment: Kaffarat, Sadaqat al-Fitr, and 'Ushr
Classification of hybrid obligations between worship and punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between pure worship, pure punishment, and hybrid obligations, and apply the Hanafi criteria for classifying 'ushr.

Core Idea
Some obligations blend worship and punishment; their hybrid nature conditions who is obligated and why.

This passage from al-Noor al-Anwar introduces a key usul concept: not all obligations fall neatly into 'pure worship' or 'pure punishment'. Some, like expiations, charity of breaking fast, and tithe on produce, combine elements of both. Understanding this hybrid category is essential for correctly deriving rulings, especially regarding who must fulfill them and when.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the passage and list the three types of obligations that are classified as 'between worship and punishment': kaffarat, sadaqat al-fitr, and 'ushr.
2
Analyze the dual aspects of each
For each category, identify the worship aspect and the punishment or burden aspect. Note how the text describes the presence of both meanings (ma'na).
Kaffarat: worship through acts (fasting, freeing slaves) but punishment because it is recompense for prohibited acts. Sadaqat al-fitr: worship as annexed to zakat but burden because obligatory on behalf of dependents. 'Ushr: burden as land tax but worship through zakat distribution and restriction to Muslims.
3
Understand the burden-worship relationship
Distinguish between 'worship containing burden' (عبادة فيها معنى المؤنة) for sadaqat al-fitr and 'burden containing worship' (مؤنة فيها معنى العبادة) for 'ushr. Note which component is primary.
Sadaqat al-fitr is primarily worship, secondarily burden. 'Ushr is primarily burden, secondarily worship.
4
Examine the ruling on 'ushr for non-Muslims
The passage states that 'ushr is only obligatory on a Muslim initially (ابتداء). Imam Muhammad allowed it to remain on a non-Muslim who acquires land from a Muslim. Identify the illa (rationale) for the initial restriction: 'ushr contains meaning of worship (qurba), and non-Muslims are not eligible for qurba.
This illustrates how the hybrid classification directly influences the hukm.
5
Apply the framework to a new case
Given a scenario (e.g., a dhimmi inherits 'ushri land from a Muslim father), determine the ruling based on the Hanafi position explained in the text.
The ruling: the land remains 'ushri, so the dhimmi must pay 'ushr, because the land's status is inherited from the Muslim owner (Imam Muhammad's view).

فصل في الأحكام بيان أقسام حقوق الله وحقوق دائرة بينهما، أي بين العبادة والعقوبة كالكفارات فإن فيها معنى العبادة من حيث إنها تؤدى بالصوم والإعتاق والإطعام والكسوة، ومعنى العقوبة من حيث إنها لم تجب ،ابتداء، بل وجبت أجزية على أفعال محرّمة صدرت عن العباد.
وعبادة فيها معنى المؤنة أي المحنة والثقل كصدقة الفطر، فإنها في أصلها عبادة ملحقة بالزكاة، ولهذا شرط لها الإغناء، ولكن فيها معنى المؤنة، ولهذا تجب عمّن يمونه وينفق
أي للحوقها بالزكاة عليه كنفسه وأولاده الصغار وعبيده المملوكين، فإنه لما مَأَنهم بالنفقة والولاية وجب أن
يمونهم بالصدقة أيضًا لدفع البلاء. ومؤنة فيها معنى العبادة كالعشر، فإنه في نفسه مؤنة للأرض التي يزرعها، ولو لم يعط العشر للسلطان لاسترد الأرض منه، وأحالها بيد آخر، ولكن فيها معنى العبادة، وهو أنه يصرف مصارف الزكاة، ولا يجب إلا على المسلم، فحمل فعلهم المزارعة على كسب الحلال الطيب.

(السنبلي)
مي
Chapter on rulings: Explanation of the divisions of the rights of Allah and rights that are between them, i.e., between worship and punishment, like expiations (kaffarat). For they contain the meaning of worship in that they are performed through fasting, freeing slaves, feeding, and clothing, and the meaning of punishment in that they were not initially obligatory, but rather became obligatory as recompense for prohibited actions committed by the servants. And worship containing the meaning of burden (mu'na), i.e., hardship and heaviness, like the charity of breaking the fast (sadaqat al-fitr). For it is in origin an act of worship annexed to zakat, and therefore sufficiency is a condition for it. But it contains the meaning of burden, and therefore it is obligatory on behalf of those whom he supports and spends on, i.e., because of its attachment to zakat, it is upon him like himself, his minor children, and his owned slaves. For since he supports them with expenditure and guardianship, it is necessary that he support them with charity as well to repel affliction. And a burden containing the meaning of worship like the tithe ('ushr). For it is in itself a burden on the land that he cultivates, and if he does not give the tithe to the ruler, the land would be taken from him and given to another. But it contains the meaning of worship, which is that it is spent in the channels of zakat, and it is only obligatory on a Muslim. So they interpret their practice of sharecropping as earning a good and lawful sustenance. ... (al-Qamar) It is not initially obligatory as acts of worship are obligatory initially. (al-Qamar) Rather, it became obligatory as a recompense, just as punishments are obligatory as recompense for one's actions. (al-Qamar) The meaning of burden: it is what is obligatory upon a man because of someone else, and it is the head of someone else, or whatever that other person needs for survival like maintenance, for it is heavy on the payer. (al-Qamar) Worship and therefore it is called worship containing burden, not as a burden containing worship. (al-Qamar) The meaning of burden: it is obligatory upon a person because of the head of someone else. (al-Qamar) Burden: upon the giver because of the productive land. (al-Qamar) The channels of zakat, for it is the zakat of produce. (al-Qamar) And it is not obligatory except on a Muslim initially, and Muhammad permitted its remaining on a non-Muslim in that if a dhimmi owns a 'ushri land from a Muslim, it remains 'ushri as it was at his possession, and the tithe is not imposed on the land of a non-Muslim initially when the function is assigned, because in it there is the meaning of nearness (to Allah), and a non-Muslim is not qualified for nearness in any way, as in al-Tahqiq. (al-Qamar) So they interpret is an answer to an implied question: you said that the tithe contains the meaning of worship, but the reality is contrary to that, for the tithe comes from cultivation, and cultivation is a cause for leaving prayer and other commanded acts, as we see farmers generally are upon that. So he answered this statement that what is meant here by the sharecropping from which the tithe is derived is that which does not cause disobedience but is free from it, and there is no doubt that it is a good and lawful earning. (al-Sunbali)
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage from al-Noor al-Anwar discusses categories that are intermediary between pure worship and pure punishment. First, kaffarat (expiations) have dual nature: they are acts of worship (fasting, freeing slaves, feeding) but they are imposed as a recompense for prohibited acts (like breaking oath, accidental killing, etc.), thus resembling punishments in origin. Second, sadaqat al-fitr is worship (like zakat) but also a burden because it is obligatory on behalf of dependents (self, children, slaves) due to the obligation of maintenance. Thus it is called 'worship containing burden'. Third, 'ushr (tithe on agricultural produce) is primarily a burden on land (failure to pay can lead to confiscation) but contains meaning of worship because it is spent like zakat and only on Muslims. The text then addresses a potential contradiction: cultivation often leads to neglecting prayer, so how can it be worship? The answer is that the valid sharecropping intended is that which does not cause disobedience. The commentator (al-Qamar) adds that 'ushr is initially only on Muslims, though Imam Muhammad allowed it to remain on a dhimmi who acquires land from a Muslim. This highlights that the class is based on the nature of the act, not the external circumstance.

Key Terms
كفارات (kaffārāt): Expiations that are acts of worship but also punishments for prohibited acts.
مؤنة (mu'na): Burden or hardship, referring to an obligation that is heavy on the payer, like sadaqat al-fitr and 'ushr.
عشر (‘ushr): Tithe on agricultural produce; considered a burden on land with an element of worship in its distribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is sadaqat al-fitr considered 'worship containing burden' (عبادة فيها معنى المؤنة)?
A) Because it is obligatory only on the rich.
B) Because it combines the reward of charity with the hardship of supporting dependents.
C) Because it is a punishment for not fasting.
D) Because it is a tax on wealth.

Key Takeaway
Hybrid obligations require analyzing both worship and punishment aspects to determine conditions such as who is obligated and why.

Classification of the Rights of Allah in Hanafi Usul
Defining categories of rights with examples and a dispute on inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the various categories of rights of Allah in Hanafi usul, including pure worship, pure punishment, expiations, burdens, and self-standing rights, and apply the classification to specific examples.

Core Idea
Rights of Allah are classified by worship, punishment, and burden – each category carries distinct legal consequences.

In Hanafi usul, the rights of Allah (huquq Allah) are not monolithic. Some are pure acts of worship, others are penalties, and still others combine elements. The classification hinges on three dimensions: ibada (worship), uquba (punishment), and mu'na (burden/expense). Understanding these categories helps jurists determine the legal nature of obligations like sadaqat al-fitr, 'ushr, kharaj, and khums. This passage from Noor al-Anwar lays out the taxonomy and also touches on a fiqh dispute about inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three core dimensions
The passage categorizes rights of Allah using three aspects: ibada (worship), uquba (punishment), and mu'na (burden). Recognize how each dimension shapes the ruling.
Mu'na refers to a financial or physical burden, often due to another person or property.
2
Learn the six subcategories of huquq Allah
Based on combinations of the three dimensions, the author lists: pure worship (e.g., obligatory prayers), pure punishment (e.g., hadd penalties), expiations (both worship and punishment), worship with burden (sadaqat al-fitr), burden with worship ('ushr), and burden with punishment (kharaj).
3
Examine the self-standing right (haqq qa'im bi nafsihi)
This category has none of the three dimensions. Examples are khums of spoils and minerals. It is a right that belongs to Allah but is collected and distributed by the ruler as deputy.
The term 'qa'im bi nafsihi' means established by itself.
4
Apply the classification to the inheritance dispute
The passage includes a ruling: a minor who kills his parent inherits according to Hanafis, but not according to Shafi'is. The Hanafi proof is that deprivation is punishment, and a minor is not subject to punishment. This illustrates how classification affects fiqh.
This is a side point showing the practical impact of the categories.
5
Distinguish rights of servants (huquq al-ibad)
Rights of servants are based on private claims (e.g., compensation for damage, blood money, sale contracts, marriage). They are not categorized by ibada/uquba/mu'na.
