And when he finished the explanation of reason, he began the explanation of legal capacity (ahliyya) which is dependent upon it, and said: [Ahliyya and its two types] (Quran 7:172). And ahliyya is of two types: the first type is capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub), which is based on the existence of legal personality (dhimma). That is, capacity for obligation is not established except after the existence of a dhimma suitable for obligation for him and upon him. And it (the dhimma) is the covenant (ʿahd) that we covenanted with our Lord on the Day of the Covenant (yawm al-mithaq) by His saying: 'Am I not your Lord?' They said: 'Yes, we testify.' So when we affirmed His lordship on the Day of the Covenant, we affirmed all His laws that are suitable for us and upon us. And the human is born with a dhimma suitable for obligation for him and upon him, based on that past covenant. And as long as he is not yet born, he is a part of the mother; he is freed by her manumission, and enters into sale as an appendage to her, and his dhimma is not suitable because the slave woman (umm al-walad) – the right of maintenance of relatives and the price of goods purchased by her guardian are obligatory upon her, while it (his dhimma) is suitable for what is due to him, such as manumission, inheritance, bequest, and lineage. And when he is born, it (the dhimma) becomes suitable for what is due to him and upon him. Harm – i.e., for his benefit – i.e., for the sake of the minor – except that obligation is not intended for itself; rather what is intended is its performance (ada'). Since that – i.e., performance – is not conceivable in the case of the minor (i.e., performing obligations by choice due to incapacity), the obligation is for his benefit and against him. That is, for the obligation of legal rulings prescribed for benefit or harm, so 'lam' is for benefit, and the word 'ala' is for harm. (al-Qamar) 'And it' i.e., the dhimma. Then know that dhimma lexically means covenant; because breaking it incurs blame. And the meaning of dhimma legally is: a self (nafs) and neck (raqaba) that has a dhimma, naming the locus by the name of the state (al-hal). Thus Fakhr al-Islam mentioned it in 'al-Tahqiq'. (al-Qamar) 'Day of the Covenant' i.e., the day when Allah Almighty took a covenant from the children of Adam to affirm His lordship, and it is the day when He brought forth all the atoms from the back of Adam (peace be upon him) in the form of ants. (al-Qamar) 'that covenant' i.e., which took place between the servant and the Lord. (al-Qamar) 'from manumission etc.' i.e., the manumission of the fetus, its inheritance from its deceased relative, a bequest to it, and the establishment of its lineage. This is an explanation of his saying: 'what is due to him.' (al-Qamar) 'was suitable' etc. So it would be necessary that all rights be due for his benefit and against him, as they are due upon the adult due to the completeness of the dhimma, except that obligation is not intended for itself – i.e., the Lawgiver does not intend it for itself. (al-Qamar) 'its performance' i.e., performing the obligatory act by choice, realizing the test (ibtilā'). (al-Qamar) 'that is not conceivable' etc.: due to the minor's inability to perform by choice. (al-Qamar) That...

Commentary
This passage defines legal capacity (ahliyya) and its types, specifically capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub). The passage type is a legal definition. It begins by stating that capacity is dependent on reason, and then introduces the two types. The first type is capacity for obligation, which is based on the existence of dhimma (legal personality). Dhimma is defined as the covenant made on the Day of the Covenant (yawm al-mithaq) mentioned in Quran 7:172, where all souls affirmed Allah's lordship, thereby accepting all His laws. A human is born with a dhimma suitable for obligations due to this pre-eternal covenant. Before birth, the fetus has no independent dhimma; it is part of the mother. The commentary explains that the dhimma is linguistically 'covenant' and legally refers to the person (nafs) that bears rights and duties. The passage then clarifies that obligation (wujub) is not an end in itself; the end is performance (ada'), which requires capacity and choice. Since a minor cannot perform obligations voluntarily, the obligations are conceptualized as either for benefit (manfa'a) or harm (madarra). The 'lam' in 'lahu' indicates benefit, and 'ala' indicates harm. The commentary by al-Qamar elaborates on these points, including that the dhimma is suitable for rights like manumission, inheritance, etc. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'أن', suggesting the explanation continues on the next page.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): The capacity (salahiyya) of a person to have rights and duties; it is of two types: capacity for obligation and capacity for performance.
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal personality or capacity to bear rights and duties; linguistically covenant, legally the person (nafs) that has a dhimma.
أهلية وجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): The capacity to have rights (benefits) and duties (harms) established for a person, based on the existence of dhimma, even if performance is not possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis (sabab) for a human having legal personality (dhimma) from birth?
A) Physical existence and ability to act.
B) The primordial covenant (mithaq) with Allah affirming His lordship.
C) The child's own explicit affirmation of faith after birth.
D) The parent's acceptance of Islamic law on behalf of the child.

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity (ahliyyat al-wujub) is derived from the primordial covenant and is inherent in every human from birth, forming the foundation for all Shari'i obligations and rights.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Definition and Basis of Ahliyyah (Legal Capacity) in Hanafi Usul
Definition of ahliyyah and its two types, with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ahliyyah, distinguish between its two types, and explain how ahliyyat al-wujub is established through the dhimmah and the Quranic covenant.

Core Idea
Every human is born with a dhimmah from the Day of Covenant, making them eligible for rights and duties.

This passage introduces the foundational concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Hanafi usul. It defines two types: capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'). We will focus on the first type, which is based on the dhimmah, a legal personality originating from the primal covenant (yawm al-mithaq) mentioned in Qur'an 7:172. Understanding this basis is crucial because it explains why even a newborn has certain rights and obligations under Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ahliyyah
The text states that ahliyyah is of two types: ahliyyat al-wujub and ahliyyat al-ada'. Note that the first type is based on the existence of the dhimmah.
Ahliyyat al-wujub is about having rights and duties; ahliyyat al-ada' is about performing actions.
2
Trace the origin of the dhimmah
The dhimmah is traced back to the Day of Covenant (yawm al-mithaq) when Allah asked all human souls 'Am I not your Lord?' and they replied 'Yes'. This acknowledgment establishes the basis for obligation.
Qur'an 7:172 is the proof.
3
Explain the status of the fetus vs. the newborn
Before birth, the fetus is part of the mother and has no independent dhimmah. After birth, the child's dhimmah becomes suitable for both rights (for him) and obligations (upon him).
The text mentions emancipation, inheritance, bequest, and lineage as rights that benefit the child even before birth, but obligations only attach after birth.
4
Define dhimmah in Hanafi terminology
Literally 'covenant', legally it means the self and neck that has a dhimmah, i.e., a legal personality that makes one subject to rights and duties.
The commentator references Fakhr al-Islam and the book al-Tahqiq.

Legal Capacity and Its Two Types
Explanation of Legal Capacity
And when he finished explaining the intellect, he began to explain the legal capacity based upon it, saying:
[Legal Capacity and Its Two Types]
(Al-A'raf: 172)
And legal capacity is of two types: The first type: capacity for obligation, and it is based on the existence of the dhimmah, meaning a capacity of the self, i.e., a capacity for obligation is not established except after the existence of a dhimmah suitable for obligation for him and upon him. And it is an expression of the covenant which our Lord covenanted with us on the Day of the Covenant by His saying: "Am I not your Lord? They said: Yes, we bear witness." So when we affirmed His Lordship on the Day of the Covenant, we affirmed all His laws suitable for us and upon us. And a human is born with a dhimmah suitable for obligation for him and upon him based on that past covenant. And as long as he is not born, he is a part of his mother, he is freed by her emancipation, and he enters into sale as a consequence of her, and his dhimmah is not suitable, because...

As for his beating etc., it is the answer to an implied question: the child is commanded to pray when he is ten years old, and if he does not comply, he is beaten, and this is evidence of his being accountable. So the commentator answered with this statement that his beating is for discipline, not for punishment, and for habit, not for obligation, i.e., so that he becomes accustomed, not because he is obligated. Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a definition and elaboration of the concept of legal capacity (الأهلية) in Hanafi legal theory. It begins by introducing two types of capacity: capacity for obligation (أهلية وجوب) and capacity for performance (أهلية أداء). The discussion focuses on the first type, explaining that it is based on the existence of the dhimmah (legal personality/covenantal obligation), which originates from the primal covenant (يوم الميثاق) mentioned in Qur'an 7:172. Every human is born with a dhimmah suitable for rights and duties due to that covenant. While a fetus has no independent dhimmah, once born, the child's dhimmah becomes valid.

The text then addresses a critical question: if the purpose of obligation is performance (أداء) and a child cannot perform (due to lack of capacity), why are any obligations imposed on the child? The Hanafi answer is that not all obligations are annulled; those that involve financial compensation or maintenance (حقوق العباد) are still binding because the guardian's performance substitutes for the child's. These include guarantee for destroyed property (ضمان المتلفات), consideration for purchases (عوض), and maintenance of relatives. However, penalties (عقوبة) and retributions (جزاء) such as retaliation (قصاص) and punishments (حدود) are not binding on the child, as they require personal accountability. Rights of Allah like tithe (عشر) and land tax (خراج) are considered burdens (مؤن) and thus apply to the child through the guardian. The commentator also clarifies that disciplinary beating for missing prayer is not a punishment but training. This nuanced position shows how Hanafi fiqh balances the child's incapacities with the need to preserve rights and public interests.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity in the sense of suitability for legal rights and duties.
أهلية وجوب (ahliyyat al-wujūb): The capacity to have rights in one's favor and obligations against one's self; it is established by the dhimmah and is present from birth.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): A legal personality or covenantal obligation that makes a person subject to rights and duties; it originates from the Day of Covenant.
يوم الميثاق (yawm al-mīthāq): The Day of Covenant when Allah took a pledge from all human souls to acknowledge His Lordship.
الصبي (al-ṣabiyy): A minor child who has not reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary basis for ahliyyat al-wujub?
A) Reaching puberty
B) The guardian's consent
C) The primal covenant (yawm al-mithaq) and the dhimmah
D) The child's ability to understand

Key Takeaway
Ahliyyat al-wujub is established for every human from the moment of birth based on the primal covenant, forming the basis for rights and certain financial duties.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
Application of Ahliyyat al-Wujub: Which Obligations Bind a Minor?
Detailed application of capacity rules to minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between financial obligations (compensation, maintenance) that bind a minor and penal obligations that do not, and explain the role of the guardian.

Core Idea
Financial rights of others bind a minor through the guardian; penalties require personal responsibility and do not apply.

After establishing that a minor has ahliyyat al-wujub from birth, this passage addresses a key question: if obligation aims at performance, and a child cannot perform, why are any obligations imposed? The Hanafi answer is that obligations are of two categories: those involving financial rights of others (e.g., compensation, maintenance) are binding because the guardian's performance substitutes for the child's; rights of Allah like tithe and land tax also apply as financial burdens. In contrast, penalties like retaliation and legal punishments are not binding because they require personal accountability. This nuanced distinction preserves societal rights while recognizing the child's incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: obligation vs. performance
The text notes that obligation is not intended for its own sake but for performance (ada'). Since a child cannot perform by choice, the obligation might theoretically be voided. But Hanafi law does not void all obligations.
The key is to see which obligations survive the child's incapacity.
2
Classify obligations into two categories
The passage divides obligations into: (1) those that are rights of people (huquq al-'ibad) involving financial compensation, such as guarantee for destroyed property (daman al-mutlifat), price of goods (thaman), and maintenance of relatives; and (2) those that are penalties ('uqubah) or retribution (jaza').
Also includes rights of Allah (huquq Allah) like 'ushr and kharaj considered as burdens (mu'an).
3
Understand why financial obligations bind the minor
Financial obligations bind because the guardian's performance (ada' al-wali) is considered like the minor's own performance. The intended thing is wealth, not the personal act.
The text says: 'the performance of his guardian is like his performance.'
4
Understand why penalties do not bind
Penalties require personal responsibility and the effect of obligation (hukm al-wujub) is the demand for punishment, which the child cannot fulfill. So the obligation becomes void.
Beating for discipline is not a penalty but training.
5
Apply to rights of Allah
Rights of Allah like 'ushr and kharaj are treated as financial burdens (mu'an) and thus bind the minor through the guardian.
The commentator notes that all fines and burdens apply to the discerning minor (al-sabiyy al-mumayyiz).

(المحشي) من المؤن: أي من مؤن الأرض. (القمر)

Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a definition and elaboration of the concept of legal capacity (الأهلية) in Hanafi legal theory. It begins by introducing two types of capacity: capacity for obligation (أهلية وجوب) and capacity for performance (أهلية أداء). The discussion focuses on the first type, explaining that it is based on the existence of the dhimmah (legal personality/covenantal obligation), which originates from the primal covenant (يوم الميثاق) mentioned in Qur'an 7:172. Every human is born with a dhimmah suitable for rights and duties due to that covenant. While a fetus has no independent dhimmah, once born, the child's dhimmah becomes valid.

The text then addresses a critical question: if the purpose of obligation is performance (أداء) and a child cannot perform (due to lack of capacity), why are any obligations imposed on the child? The Hanafi answer is that not all obligations are annulled; those that involve financial compensation or maintenance (حقوق العباد) are still binding because the guardian's performance substitutes for the child's. These include guarantee for destroyed property (ضمان المتلفات), consideration for purchases (عوض), and maintenance of relatives. However, penalties (عقوبة) and retributions (جزاء) such as retaliation (قصاص) and punishments (حدود) are not binding on the child, as they require personal accountability. Rights of Allah like tithe (عشر) and land tax (خراج) are considered burdens (مؤن) and thus apply to the child through the guardian. The commentator also clarifies that disciplinary beating for missing prayer is not a punishment but training. This nuanced position shows how Hanafi fiqh balances the child's incapacities with the need to preserve rights and public interests.

Key Terms
ضمان المتلفات (daman al-mutlifat): Guarantee (compensation) for destroyed property; a financial obligation that binds the minor.
العقوبة (al-'uqubah): Penalty or punishment; in this context, specifically retaliation (qisas) and not hudud or deprivation of inheritance.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): Retribution or compensation for harm caused by the child's action (e.g., beating).
العشر والخراج (al-'ushr wa al-kharaj): Tithe and land tax; considered financial burdens (mu'an) that apply to the minor.
المميز (al-mumayyiz): A discerning minor who can distinguish between beneficial and harmful actions; typically around age 7-10.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is retaliation (qisas) not binding on a minor who causes harm?
A) Because retaliation is a penalty that requires personal responsibility, which the minor cannot fulfill
B) Because the minor cannot afford compensation
C) Because retaliation is a right of Allah, not a right of the victim
D) Because the guardian always takes responsibility

Key Takeaway
A minor is financially liable for rights of others (via guardian) but not for penalties, since penalties require personal accountability.

Financial Obligations from Rights of Servants Bind a Minor
Usul rule on obligations binding a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi rule that financial obligations from rights of servants bind a minor, and DISTINGUISH them from punishments.

Core Idea
Financial obligations from rights of servants bind the minor; punishments do not.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar lays out a foundational usul principle: a minor is liable for financial claims arising from the rights of individuals, but not for punishments or penalties. The key distinction turns on the purpose of the obligation — compensation vs. requital. We will unpack the rule and apply it to concrete cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of obligations
The text divides obligations into rights of servants (huquq al-ibad) and rights of Allah (huquq Allah). Within rights of servants, it further separates financial claims (gharam, 'iwad) from punishments ('uqubah, jaza).
This classification is the backbone of the ruling.
2
Extract the rule for rights of servants
For rights of servants: 'ma kan min huquq al-ibad min al-gharam... lazimahu' — whatever is a financial indemnity or compensation is binding on the minor. The guardian's performance stands in for the minor's.
The text explicitly mentions daman al-mutlifat (guarantee for damages), thaman (price), and nafaqah (maintenance).
3
Examine what constitutes 'punishment'
The commentator clarifies that 'uqubah here means qisas (retaliation), not hudud or other penalties. This restriction avoids internal contradiction and aligns the rule with the principle that minors are not liable for requital.
Disciplinary beating is excluded — it is ta'dib (discipline), not jaza (penalty).
4
Understand the role of the guardian
The guardian's payment is treated as the minor's performance because the objective is financial compensation, not personal action. The obligation is thus not devoid of its ruling (hukm).
This reasoning shows that the binding force depends on achievability through a representative.

It is permissible for obligation to be nullified due to lack of its ruling. Whatever from the rights of servants regarding indemnity like guarantee of destroyed property, and compensation like the price of sold goods, and maintenance of wives and relatives, is binding upon him, and the performance of his guardian is like his performance, and the obligation is not devoid of its ruling. Whatever is a punishment or a penalty is not binding upon him. It is intended that 'punishment' here means retaliation, and 'penalty' means the requital of the act issuing from him through beating and inflicting pain, excluding hudud and deprivation of inheritance, so that it corresponds to the rights of Allah the Exalted being excluded from them. As for his beating when misbehaving, it is from the category of discipline, not from the types of penalty. And the rights of Allah the Exalted are binding whenever the ruling concerning them is valid, like tithe and land tax, for they are originally from the expenses, and the meaning of worship is upon the minor, and it is performance, and punishment is subsidiary in both, and the intent from both is wealth, and the performance of the guardian in that is like his performance... (glosses follow).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal capacity (ahliyah) of a minor (sabi). The commentator explains that obligations are divided into two categories: rights of servants (huquq al-ibad) and rights of Allah (huquq Allah). Under rights of servants, financial obligations such as guarantee for damages (daman al-mutlifat), payment of price (thaman), and maintenance (nafaqah) for wives and relatives are binding on the minor. The guardian's performance is equivalent to the minor's performance because the goal is financial compensation, not personal action. However, punishments (uqubah) and penalties (jaza) are not binding because the minor is not subject to accountability for requital. The commentator clarifies that 'punishment' here refers specifically to retaliation (qisas), not to hudud or deprivation of inheritance, as those are rights of Allah. The beating of a child for failing to pray is disciplinary (ta'dib), not penal. Regarding rights of Allah, obligations like tithe (ushr) and land tax (kharaj) are binding when valid because they are essentially financial burdens, not pure acts of worship. Pure acts of worship like prayer and fasting require intention (niyyah) and voluntary performance, which a minor cannot fulfill validly. Similarly, punishments like hudud are not required because they entail requital, which is inappropriate for a minor. The passage also notes that a disbeliever is not obligated with acts of worship because they are not eligible for reward.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyah): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations
صبي (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty
حقوق العباد (huquq al-ibad): Rights of individuals (servants) involving financial claims
حقوق الله (huquq Allah): Rights of Allah, including acts of worship and punishments
عقوبة (uqubah): Punishment, interpreted here as retaliation (qisas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following obligations is binding on a minor?
A) Payment of the price of an item purchased
B) Performance of the five daily prayers
C) Payment of blood money (diyah) for intentional murder
D) Fasting in Ramadan

Key Takeaway
Financial obligations from rights of servants bind the minor; punishments (qisas) do not; rights of Allah are binding only when the purpose is financial.

Rights of Allah: When They Bind a Minor
Usul rule on rights of Allah binding a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to DISTINGUISH between rights of Allah that bind a minor (financial) and those that do not (pure worship and punishments), and EXPLAIN the reasoning based on the purpose of the obligation.

Core Idea
Financial rights of Allah (ushr, kharaj) bind the minor; pure worship and punitive rights do not.

The previous lesson established that financial obligations from rights of servants bind a minor. Now we turn to rights of Allah (huquq Allah). The passage teaches that not all rights of Allah are equal: those that are financial in nature, like tithe (ushr) and land tax (kharaj), are binding because the goal is wealth, not personal worship. Pure acts of worship and punishments are exempt due to the minor's lack of capacity for personal performance and requital.

Guided Path
1
Recall the classification of rights of Allah
Rights of Allah include acts of worship (e.g., prayer, fasting) and financial obligations (e.g., ushr, kharaj) and punishments (e.g., hudud). The passage focuses on the financial ones.
The commentator separates pure worship from financial rights.
2
Identify the rule for financial rights of Allah
The text says: 'wa huquq Allah ta'ala tajibu mata saha al-qawl bi hukmihi k'al-ushr wa al-kharaj' – they are binding when valid. The reason is that they are fundamentally expenses (mu'an) with a subsidiary aspect of worship.
The meaning of worship is attached to the minor, but the main goal is wealth.
3
Understand why pure worship does not bind
Pure acts of worship require personal performance (al-ada) with intention. The minor lacks the capacity for this, so the obligation is null (batala al-wujub).
The passage also mentions that a disbeliever is not obligated because they are not eligible for reward.
4
Note the exclusion of punitive rights of Allah
The passage earlier restricted 'punishment' to qisas (rights of servants). Hudud and other punitive rights of Allah are also not binding on the minor because they involve requital (mu'akhadhah), which the minor is not subject to.
This is consistent with the principle that minors are not liable for punishment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فجاز أن يبطل الوجوب لعدم حكمه، فما كان من حقوق العباد من الغرم كضمان المتلفات، والعوض كثمن المبيع، ونفقة الزوجات والأقارب لزمه، ويكون أداء وليه كأدائه، وكان الوجوب غير خال عن حكمه. وما كان عقوبة أو جزاء لم يجب عليه، ينبغي أن يراد "بالعقوبة" ههنا قصاص، و"بالجزاء" جزاء الفعل الصادر منه بالضرب والإيلام دون الحدود وحرمان الميراث ليكون مقابلاً لحقوق أي العقوبة والجزاء الله تعالى خارجة عنها. وأما ضربه عند إساءة الأدب فمن باب التأديب، لا من أنواع الجزاء. وحقوق الله تعالى تجب متى صح القول بحكمه كالعشر والخراج، فإنهما في الأصل من المؤن، ومعني العبادة أي على الصبي وهو الأداء والعقوبة تابع فيهما، وإنما المقصود منهما: المال، وأداء الولي في ذلك كأدائه. النفس الفعل صفة لعدم حكمه أي لعدم حكم الوجوب وهو الأداء، ولذا لا يجب على الكافر شيء من الشرائع التي هي الطاعات، فإن حكم الوجوب الأداء، وفائدة الأداء نيل الثواب في الآخرة حكمًا من الله تعالى والكافر مع الكفر ليس أهلاً للثواب عقوبةً له، كذا قيل. (القمر) فما كان إلخ شروع في تفصيل الأحكام المشروعة بأن أيّ حكم يلزم الصبي وأيّ حكم لا يلزمه. (القمر) كضمان المتلفات بأن انقلب الطفل على مال إنسان فأتلفه يجب عليه الضمان. (القمر) والعوض بالجر معطوف على المجرور في قوله من الغرم. (القمر) والأقارب في "التلويح": إن نفقة الأقارب صلة تشبه المؤنة من جهة أنها تجب على الغني كفاية لما يحتاج إليه، بخلاف نفقة الزوجة، فإنها تشبه الأعواض من جهة أنها وجبت جزاءً للاحتباس الواجب عليها عند الرجل. (القمر) لزمه أي لزم الصبي وإن كان لا يعقل. (القمر) كأدائه أي كأداء الصبي؛ لأن المقصود ههنا المال لا نفس الفعل، فيجزي أداء الولي نيابة (القمر) وما كان عقوبة كان يرد عليه لعل المراد بالعقوبة الحدود وحرمان الميراث؛ لأنها ظاهرة فيهما، وهما المتبادران منها، وعلى هذا فلا يصح تقابل هذا الكلام. (السنبلي) لم يجب عليه أي على الصبي؛ لأنه لا يصلح لحكم الوجوب، وهو المطالبة بالعقوبة وجزاء الفعل فبطل الوجوب . (القمر) دون إلخ أي ليس المراد بالجزاء: الحدود وحرمان الميراث بسبب قتل المورث. وأما ضربه إلخ جواب سؤال مقدر تقديره أن الصبي يؤمر بأداء الصلاة وهو ابن عشر سنة، فإن لم يمتثل فيضرب عليه، وهو دليل كونه مكلّفا، فأجاب الشارح بهذا القول بأن ضربه لأجل التأديب لا لأجل التعذيب، وللاعتياد لا للتكليف أي لكي يعتاد لا لأنه مكلف. (السنبلي) وحقوق الله تعالى تجب إلخ: لأن الحدود أيضا من حقوق الله تعالى، فلذا دفعه الشارح بقوله : ينبغي أن يراد إلخ. (السنبلي) والخراج وكذا جميع الغرامات والمؤنات تجب على الصبي المميز . (المحشي) من المؤن: أي من مؤن الأرض. (القمر) عنه وهو الأداء ومتى بطل القول بحكمه لا تجب كالعبادات الخالصة والعقوبات، فإن المقصود من العبادات فعل الأداء، ولا يتصوّر ذلك في الصبي. والمقصود من العقوبات: هو المؤاخذة بالفعل، وهو لا يصلح لذلك. أي للمؤاخذة بالفعل
It is permissible for obligation to be nullified due to lack of its ruling. Whatever from the rights of servants regarding indemnity like guarantee of destroyed property, and compensation like the price of sold goods, and maintenance of wives and relatives, is binding upon him, and the performance of his guardian is like his performance, and the obligation is not devoid of its ruling. Whatever is a punishment or a penalty is not binding upon him. It is intended that 'punishment' here means retaliation, and 'penalty' means the requital of the act issuing from him through beating and inflicting pain, excluding hudud and deprivation of inheritance, so that it corresponds to the rights of Allah the Exalted being excluded from them. As for his beating when misbehaving, it is from the category of discipline, not from the types of penalty. And the rights of Allah the Exalted are binding whenever the ruling concerning them is valid, like tithe and land tax, for they are originally from the expenses, and the meaning of worship is upon the minor, and it is performance, and punishment is subsidiary in both, and the intent from both is wealth, and the performance of the guardian in that is like his performance... (glosses follow).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the legal capacity (ahliyah) of a minor (sabi). The commentator explains that obligations are divided into two categories: rights of servants (huquq al-ibad) and rights of Allah (huquq Allah). Under rights of servants, financial obligations such as guarantee for damages (daman al-mutlifat), payment of price (thaman), and maintenance (nafaqah) for wives and relatives are binding on the minor. The guardian's performance is equivalent to the minor's performance because the goal is financial compensation, not personal action. However, punishments (uqubah) and penalties (jaza) are not binding because the minor is not subject to accountability for requital. The commentator clarifies that 'punishment' here refers specifically to retaliation (qisas), not to hudud or deprivation of inheritance, as those are rights of Allah. The beating of a child for failing to pray is disciplinary (ta'dib), not penal. Regarding rights of Allah, obligations like tithe (ushr) and land tax (kharaj) are binding when valid because they are essentially financial burdens, not pure acts of worship. Pure acts of worship like prayer and fasting require intention (niyyah) and voluntary performance, which a minor cannot fulfill validly. Similarly, punishments like hudud are not required because they entail requital, which is inappropriate for a minor. The passage also notes that a disbeliever is not obligated with acts of worship because they are not eligible for reward.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyah): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations
صبي (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty
حقوق العباد (huquq al-ibad): Rights of individuals (servants) involving financial claims
حقوق الله (huquq Allah): Rights of Allah, including acts of worship and punishments
عقوبة (uqubah): Punishment, interpreted here as retaliation (qisas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following rights of Allah is binding on a minor according to the passage?
A) Payment of zakat al-fitr
B) Offering voluntary prayers
C) Payment of land tax (kharaj)
D) Fasting in Ramadan

Key Takeaway
Financial rights of Allah (ushr, kharaj) bind the minor because they are expenses; pure worship and punitive rights do not.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Understanding Capacity for Performance: Deficient vs. Complete
Defining deficient and complete capacity for performing acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ahliyah al-ada, distinguish between its deficient and complete types, and explain the legal effect of each type.

Core Idea
Deficient capacity makes acts valid; complete capacity makes acts obligatory.

In this passage, we continue our study of legal capacity (ahliyah) by examining the capacity for performing acts (ahliyah al-ada). Unlike capacity for obligation (ahliyah al-wujub), which determines whether one is addressed by divine commands, capacity for performance determines whether one's acts are legally recognized. The Hanafi school divides this capacity into two: deficient and complete. Understanding this division is crucial for determining when a minor's or an insane person's worship is valid, and when obligations become binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
State the definition of ahliyah al-ada: the capacity that makes one's performance of acts legally valid. It is based on two sub-capacities: understanding the divine address (intellect) and acting upon it (body).
This is not the same as capacity for obligation.
2
Distinguish the Two Types
Recognize that deficient capacity (qasirah) arises when either intellect or body is deficient, while complete capacity (kamilah) requires both to be sound and mature.
Examples: intelligent minor (sound mind, weak body) and insane adult (weak mind, strong body) both have deficient capacity.
3
Understand the Effect of Deficient Capacity
If a person with deficient capacity performs an act, it is valid (sahih) but not obligatory. That is, they are not commanded to perform, but if they choose to, the act counts.
This distinguishes them from those with complete capacity, for whom the act is both valid and obligatory.
4
Understand the Effect of Complete Capacity
Complete capacity makes the act both valid and obligatory. The person is a mukallaf and is sinful for omission.
This applies to a mature, sane adult.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الثاني : أهلية أداء وهي نوعان قاصرة تبتني على القدرة القاصرة من العقل القاصر والبدن القاصر، فإن الأداء يتعلق بقدرتين: قدرة فهم الخطاب، وهي بالعقل، أي بالعقل والبدن وقدرة العمل به، وهي بالبدن، فإذا كان تحقق القدرة بهما يكون كمالها بكمالهما أي بالخطاب العقل والبدن وقصورها بقصورهما، فالإنسان في أوّل أحواله عديم القدرتين، ولكن له استعدادهما، فتحصلان له شيئًا فشيئًا إلى أن يبلغ كالصبي العاقل، فإن بدنه قاصر وإن كان عقله يحتمل الكمال، والمعتوه البالغ فإن عقله قاصر وإن كان بدنه كاملاً، وتبتني عليها، أي على الأهلية القاصرة صحة الأداء على معنى أنه لوأدّى يكون صحيحا وإن لم يجب عليه. وكاملة: تبتني على القدرة الكاملة من العقل الكامل والبدن الكامل . أي الناشية من العقل لا تجب: أي على المولود حقوق الله تعالى كالعبادات الخالصة أي التي لا تؤدّى ولا تصح إلا بالنية كالصلاة والزكاة والعقوبات كالحدود. (القمر) فإن المقصود من العبادات إلخ قيل والزكاة وإن تتأدى بالنائب لكن إيجابها للابتلاء بالأداء بالاختيار، وليس الصبي من أهلهما. (القمر) فعل الأداء إلخ: وهو موقوف على النية، ولا تمكن النية من الصبي، بخلاف العشر والخراج؛ فإنهما لا يحتاجان إلى النية فإن المقصود منهما المال لا الفعل ليكون موقوفا على النية.(السنبلي ولا يتصور ذلك إلخ لعجز الصبي عن الأداء بالاختيار.(القمر) هو المؤاخذة بالفعل كجزاء جناية الإحرام وكفارة نقض الصوم. (القمر) أهلية أداء أي أهلية أداء العبادات بحيث لو أداها يعتد بها شرعًا. (القمر) من العقل: أي الناشئة من العقل. (القمر) بقصورهما : وكذا بانتفاء أحد القدرتين.(المحشي) عديم القدرتين أي قدرة فهم الخطاب وقدرة العمل بالخطاب. (القمر) قاصر: أي من احتمال الأفعال الشاقة. (القمر) والمعتوه العته: آفة توجب خللاً في العقل فيصير صاحبه مختلط الكلام ومختلط الأفعال. (القمر) فإن عقله : لأنه بمنزلة الصبي، فإنه عاقل لم يعتدل عقله. (المحشي)
And the second type: capacity for performance (ahliyah al-ada), and it is of two types: deficient (qasirah) built upon deficient capacity from deficient intellect and deficient body. For performance is related to two capacities: capacity to understand address (khitab), which is by intellect, and capacity to act upon it, which is by body. So when the capacities are realized through both, their perfection is by the perfection of both, and their deficiency is by the deficiency of both. Thus a human in his initial state is lacking both capacities, but he has the potential for them, so he acquires them gradually until he reaches majority, like the intelligent minor, whose body is deficient although his intellect can attain perfection, and the insane adult, whose intellect is deficient although his body is complete. Upon it, i.e., upon the deficient capacity, is founded the validity of performance in the sense that if he performs, it is valid even if it was not obligatory upon him. And complete (kamilah): built upon complete capacity from complete intellect and complete body. (Glosses follow: 'does not obligate: i.e., upon the newborn the rights of Allah...' etc.)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of legal capacity: ahliyah al-ada (capacity for performance). It is divided into deficient (qasirah) and complete (kamilah). Deficient capacity is based on limited mental or physical ability. Performance (ada) requires two capacities: understanding the divine address (khitab) which comes from the intellect, and the ability to act on that address, which comes from the body. A person may have one but not the other (e.g., an intelligent minor has a sound mind but weak body; an insane adult has a strong body but weak mind). Although such a person is not obligated to perform acts of worship, if they do perform them, the acts are valid. Complete capacity requires both sound intellect and body (e.g., a mature, sane adult). The glosses further clarify that pure acts of worship like prayer and zakat are not obligatory on a minor because they require intention (niyyah), which a minor cannot have validly. However, financial obligations like ushr and kharaj are exempt from this requirement and thus may be binding even on a minor if they are a landowner.

Key Terms
أهلية أداء (ahliyah al-ada): Capacity to perform religious acts validly; it is of two types: deficient and complete.
قاصرة (qasirah): Deficient capacity: based on either limited intellect or limited physical ability.
كاملة (kamilah): Complete capacity: based on both sound intellect and mature physical ability.
قدرة فهم الخطاب (qudrat fahm al-khitab): Capacity to understand the divine address (Quran and Sunnah), rooted in intellect.
قدرة العمل به (qudrat al-'amal bihi): Capacity to physically perform the commanded actions, rooted in the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) An adult who is mentally sound and physically healthy
B) An intelligent minor with a weak body
C) An adult who is asleep
D) A newborn infant

Key Takeaway
Ahliyah al-ada is divided into deficient (valid but not obligatory) and complete (valid and obligatory), based on the perfection of intellect and body.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Legal Capacity and the First Two Divisions of Rulings for Minors
Explaining legal capacity and the first two categories of rulings for minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of ahliyyah to determine the validity and binding effect of a minor's acts of faith and apostasy, and evaluate the ikhtilaf with other schools.

Core Idea
A minor’s acts of pure benefit (iman) or pure harm (kufr) are valid by istihsan, but benefit is not binding until puberty.

This passage explores the foundational usul concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and its two types: ahliyyat al-wujub and ahliyyat al-ada. Using the example of a minor, it introduces the sixfold division of rulings and details the first two: purely good acts (iman) and purely evil acts (kufr/riddah). The Hanafi position, supported by istihsan and the precedent of 'Ali (ra), is contrasted with the opposing views of al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this illustrates how the Hanafi school balances textual evidence and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define legal capacity and its two types
Identify the two types of ahliyyah – ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for obligation) and ahliyyat al-ada (capacity for performance). Understand that puberty is the marker for full capacity due to practical facilitation.
The passage establishes that rulings for a minor are based on deficient capacity, not full capacity.
2
Understand the six-division framework
Recognize that the author divides rulings for minors into six categories based on the nature of the act (good/evil) and whether it can accept its opposite. The first two deal with acts that are purely good or purely evil.
This framework helps determine when a minor’s acts have legal effect.
3
Analyze the first division: iman
The text rules that a minor’s iman is valid (sahih) but not binding (la yulzim). Identify the dalil: istihsan based on 'Ali (ra) accepting Islam as a child. Contrast with Shafi'i who holds it invalid for worldly rulings due to qiyas (harm)
The key consequence: Hanafi allows inheritance from non-Muslim father and separation from non-Muslim spouse.
4
Analyze the second division: riddah
For apostasy (pure evil), Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule it valid for both worldly and otherworldly matters, except execution. Note the istihsan reasoning: the minor is subject to harm. Compare with Abu Yusuf and Shafi'i who see it as void.
Practical effects: marriage dissolution and non-inheritance, but no killing because no actual combat.
5
Evaluate the role of istihsan and thamarah al-khilaf
Reflect on how istihsan overrides qiyas in this case. Identify the practical difference (thamarah) between the schools: Hanafi affects inheritance and marital status; Shafi'i does not until majority.
This highlights a core Hanafi usul principle: preference for benefit/harm over strict analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الأهلية
الأهلية ونوعيها ويبتني عليها وجوب الأداء وتوجّه الخطاب؛ لأن في إلزام الأداء قبل الكمال يكون حرجًا، وهو مُنتف. ولما لم يكن إدراك كماله إلا بعد تجربة عظيمة أقام الشارع البلوغ الذي يعتدل عنده العقل في الأغلب مقام اعتدال العقل تيسيرًا. والأحكام منقسمة في هذا الباب أي باب ابتناء صحة الأداء على الأهلية القاصرة دون الأهلية الكاملة التي ذكرت عن قريب إلى ستة أقسام أشار المصنف إليها على الترتيب، فقال: فحق الله تعالى إن كان حسنًا لا يحتمل غيره كالإيمان القول بصحته من الصبي بلا لزوم أداء، وهذا هو القسم الأول، وإنما قلنا: "بصحته" لأن عليا له افتخر بذلك وقال: شعر سبقتكم إلى الإسلام طرا غلامًا ما بلغت أوان حلم... وعند الشافعي لا يصح إيمانه قبل البلوغ في حق أحكام الدنيا، فيرث أباه الكافر، ولا تَبَيْن منه امرأته المشركة؛ لأنه ضررٌ وإن صح في حق أحكام الآخرة؛ لأنه محض نفع. وإنما قلنا: "بلا لزوم أداء"؛ لأنه لو استوصف الصبي ولم يصف الإسلام بعد ما عقل لم تبن امرأته ولو لزمه الأداء لكان امتناعه كفرًا.
وإن كان قبيحًا لا يحتمل غيره كالكفر لا يجعل عفوًا، وهذا هو القسم الثاني، والمراد بالكفر: هو الردّة، يعني لو ارتدّ الصبي تعتبر ردّته عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد في حق أحكام الدنيا والآخرة حتى تبين منه امرأته، ولا يرث من أقاربه المسلمين، ولكن لا يقتل؛ لأنه لم توجد منه المحاربة قبل البلوغ، ولو قتله أحد يهدر دمه، ولا يجب عليه شيء كالمرتد، وعند أبي يوسف والشافعي لا تصح ردّته. (ثم ذكر بقية الأقسام) ...
Explanation of legal capacity. Legal capacity and its two types: upon it are built the obligation of performance and the direction of address, because imposing performance before completion would be hardship, which is negated. Since the perception of one's completeness only occurs after great experience, the Lawgiver established puberty, at which the intellect generally balances, in the place of balanced intellect for facilitation. In this chapter, rulings are divided—meaning the chapter on the building of correct performance upon deficient capacity, not upon the complete capacity mentioned earlier—into six divisions that the author indicated in order. He said: So if the right of Allah Almighty is good and cannot accept its opposite, like faith, we rule its validity from the minor without binding performance—this is the first division. We said 'its validity' because 'Ali boasted of that and said: 'I preceded you all to Islam as a boy not having reached the age of dream...' And according to al-Shafi'i, his faith is not valid before puberty in terms of worldly rulings, so he inherits from his disbelieving father, and his polytheist wife does not separate from him, because it is harm, although it is valid in terms of the afterlife because it is pure benefit. And we said 'without binding performance', because if the minor is asked and does not describe Islam after he has comprehended, his wife does not separate, and if performance were binding, his refusal would be disbelief.
And if it is ugly and cannot accept its opposite, like disbelief, it is not considered pardoned—this is the second division. The meaning of disbelief here is apostasy. So if a minor apostatizes, his apostasy is considered valid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad in terms of worldly and afterlife rulings, so his wife separates from him, he does not inherit from his Muslim relatives, but he is not killed because he did not commit fighting before puberty, and if someone kills him, his blood is wasted, and nothing is obligatory upon him, like an apostate. And according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, his apostasy is not valid.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and its two types: capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada). It then introduces the sixfold division of rulings concerning a minor's acts. The first division: when the act is purely good (like iman), the Hanafi position is that it is valid but not binding on the minor, until puberty. This is proven by istihsan and the report of 'Ali accepting Islam as a child. The second division: when the act is purely evil (like kufr/riddah), Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it valid as well (istihsan), affecting inheritance and marital status, but the minor is not executed because killing is contingent on actual combat. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold it invalid on qiyas. The passage clarifies that these disputes concern worldly rulings; for the afterlife, both iman and kufr of a minor are valid by consensus.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity: the quality by virtue of which one is fit for receiving obligations and executing actions.
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty.
الإيمان (al-iman): Faith, specifically the declaration of belief in Islam.
الردّة (al-riddah): Apostasy from Islam, considered the pure evil act.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference, used here to validate the minor's iman and riddah despite qiyas to the contrary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the effect of a minor's apostasy (riddah) on his marriage and inheritance?
A) His marriage remains valid and he inherits from Muslim relatives.
B) His marriage is dissolved and he does not inherit from Muslim relatives.
C) His apostasy is void and has no legal effect.
D) He is executed for apostasy.

Key Takeaway
A minor's acts of pure benefit (iman) and pure harm (kufr/riddah) are valid by istihsan, but benefit is not binding until puberty; harm takes effect except execution.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Classification of Acts According to a Minor's Legal Capacity
Classifying acts as benefit, harm, or mixed for a minor's legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify acts into pure benefit, pure harm, and mixed, and determine whether a discerning minor can perform them with or without guardian consent.

Core Idea
A minor's acts are evaluated by benefit and harm: pure benefit alone is valid, pure harm is generally invalid, and mixed requires guardian oversight.

This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work explains how legal capacity (ahlīyah) applies to a discerning minor (sābī ʿāqil). It categorizes acts into three types and explains the role of the guardian. Understanding these categories is essential for knowing when a minor's transactions are valid or void, and how the school handles exceptional cases like divorce by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of acts
The text divides acts into pure benefit (nafʿ maḥḍ), pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ), and mixed (dāʾir bayn al-amrayn). Learn the defining features of each.
Pure benefit brings only worldly or religious gain with no harm; pure harm brings no worldly benefit; mixed can be either depending on context.
2
State the ruling for each category when performed by a discerning minor
For pure benefit, the minor's act is valid without guardian consent. For pure harm, it is generally invalid but exceptions exist (e.g., divorce when necessary). For mixed, the minor may act only with guardian approval.
The discerning minor has deficient capacity (ahlīyah qāṣirah) sufficient for benefit but not for harm or mixed without support.
3
Recognize the exceptions to pure harm
The text notes that divorce by a minor is permissible when necessity arises, such as when a spouse apostatizes or is impotent. This is based on rulings by Shams al-A'imma and others.
Some Hanafi authorities allow ṭalāq by a minor in certain cases; this is a point of intra-madhhab ikhtilāf.
4
Understand the role of the guardian in mixed acts
In mixed acts like sales, the guardian's opinion tips the balance toward benefit. The minor's transaction is valid with guardian consent, even if it involves a loss (ghubn fāḥish) with outsiders, according to Abu Hanifa.
The guardian's involvement compensates for the minor's deficient capacity.

عند أبي حنيفة الله قلت: هذا باتفاق الروايات، وأما تصرّفه بالغبن الفاحش مع الولي ففي رواية يملك الصبي، وفي أخرى لا؛ لأن الولي حينئذ منهم في الإذن لجواز أن إذنه كان خداعا منه لأخذ ماله، ولا كذلك في الأجنبي :
Statement of legal capacity. Capacity and its two types. With respect to the rulings of this world; because it is pure harm, and we only ruled the validity of his faith because it is pure benefit. And what is between the two matters, i.e., between being good at one time and bad at another, and this is the third section, like prayer and similar acts: the discerning minor performs them validly without incurring liability or guarantee. If he starts them, it is not obligatory to complete them, and if he spoils them, he is not obligated to make up. This performance is pure benefit for him because he becomes accustomed to performing them, so it will not be difficult after puberty. And what is from other than the rights of Allah, if it is pure benefit like accepting a gift or charity, its direct performance by the minor is valid without the guardian's consent or permission, and this is the fourth section. And as for pure harm that has no worldly benefit mixed in, like divorce, bequest, and similar acts of manumission... (marginal notes continue).
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary explains the classification of acts according to a minor's legal capacity (ahlīyah). The author divides acts into categories: pure benefit, pure harm, and mixed. For a discerning minor (sābī ʿāqil), pure benefit acts like accepting gifts are valid without guardian approval, while pure harm acts like divorce are generally invalid unless necessity arises, as in the case of apostasy or impotence where divorce is permitted by some Hanafi authorities. Mixed acts like sales require the guardian's opinion to tilt the balance toward benefit. The text also notes exceptions, such as the validity of a minor's divorce when need arises, based on Shams al-A'imma's ruling. Marginal glosses from al-Qamar and al-Sanabīlī provide additional commentary on the reasoning and differences within the school.

Key Terms
صبي عاقل (sābī ʿāqil): A minor who has reached the age of discernment (tamyīz), capable of understanding the nature of transactions but still lacking complete legal capacity.
أهلية قاصرة (ahlīyah qāṣirah): Deficient or incomplete legal capacity that suffices for pure benefit acts but not for harmful or mixed ones without guardian involvement.
ضرر محض (ḍarar maḥḍ): Pure harm, an act that brings no worldly benefit to the minor, such as divorce or bequest, and thus is generally invalid for a minor.
نفع محض (nafʿ maḥḍ): Pure benefit, an act that only benefits the minor without any harm, like accepting a gift or prayer, and is valid even without guardian consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a minor's prayer valid without requiring completion or recompense?
A) Because prayer is a purely harmful act that the minor endures.
B) Because prayer is a pure benefit act, and the minor becomes accustomed to it.
C) Because the guardian must approve all acts of worship.
D) Because prayer is a financial transaction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of acts by benefit and harm determines whether a minor's action is valid independently, requires guardian consent, or is void except for necessity.

The Minor's Divorce: Valid When Need Arises
Hanafi usul ruling: exception to void acts of a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a minor's divorce is valid when necessity demands, and distinguish it from a minor's other void acts.

Core Idea
A minor's divorce is valid when needed, because the right to divorce is inherent in marriage.

In Hanafi usul, most dispositive acts of a minor are void because they involve pure harm (darar mahd). But divorce is an exception. This lesson explores Shams al-A'imma's reasoning that the right to divorce is a necessary concomitant of marriage ownership, and only its exercise may cause harm—not the right itself. When hardship arises, the harm is mitigated by necessity, making the minor's divorce effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Start with the baseline: a minor's acts like charity, gift, and loan are void because they remove ownership without any benefit returning. This is the principle of darar mahd.
The text says 'void originally' (yabtul aṣlan).
2
Spot the exception
Divorce is singled out as an exception. Shams al-A'imma states it is valid when need arises. Determine what constitutes 'need' (hāja).
Examples: wife’s conversion, apostasy, impotence.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta‘līl)
The right to divorce is a necessary part of marriage ownership (lāzimat milk al-nikāḥ). Harm lies only in the act of divorcing, not in the capacity itself. Thus the capacity is not harmful per se.
This distinguishes divorce from pure gifts which have no such necessity.
4
Apply to the given scenarios
In each scenario—wife converts and husband refuses Islam, apostasy, impotence—the wife faces hardship. The court’s separation is deemed a divorce, and it is valid because need justifies it.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad agree on the first scenario; Muhammad alone on apostasy.
5
Extract the usul principle
The rule: A minor’s divorce is valid (wāqi‘) when necessity demands, despite the general voidness of his dispositive acts. This is the 'fifth category' of a minor's actions.
This principle overrides the presumption of voidness.

Source Text

(القمر) واقع كيف، فإن ملك الطلاق من لوازم ملك النكاح، وليس ضرر في ملك الطلاق، إنما الضرر في إيقاع الطلاق، فالصبي يملك تطليقه ويقع طلاقه إذا دعت إلخ. (القمر) إذا دعت إليه حاجة إلخ : قاله الإمام شمس الأئمة رادا لمن زعم أن حكم الطلاق غير مشروع أصلاً حتى أن امرأته لا يكون محل الطلاق بل هي في ذلك كالأجنبية، وتقع الضرورة إذا نشأت من الزوجة مضرات عظيمة، فلا ضرر حينئذٍ في الإيقاع، وقال البحر فإن هذا القول أشبه بالصواب والله أعلم بالصواب. (السنبلي) وهو : أي التفريق طلاق عند أبي حنيفة محله (القمر) وهو أي هذه الفرقة طلاق عند محمد له. (القمر) مجبوبًا: أي مقطوع الذكر والخصيتين كذا قال العيني. (القمر)
And charity, gift, and loan are void originally, because they involve removal of ownership without benefit returning to him, but divorce and its likes. Shams al-A'imma said: The divorce of a minor is effective when need arises. Do you not see that when his wife embraces Islam, he is presented with Islam, and if he refuses, separation is made between them, and it is divorce according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And when he apostatizes, separation occurs between him and his wife, and it is divorce according to Muhammad. And if he is impotent and his wife sues him and seeks separation, that is divorce according to some. So it is known that the ruling of divorce is established in times of need, and this is the fifth section.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage classifies actions of a minor into categories. The author establishes that acts of pure harm like charity, gift, and loan are void because they remove ownership without any benefit returning. However, divorce is different: it is not inherently harmful because the right to divorce is a necessary concomitant of the right to marriage. The harm lies only in the actual pronouncement, not in the capacity itself. Shams al-A'imma argues that a minor's divorce is valid when necessity demands it, citing examples: if the minor's wife converts to Islam and he refuses, the judge separates them, and this separation is considered a divorce according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Similarly, if the minor apostatizes, the separation is a divorce according to Muhammad. If the minor is impotent and his wife seeks separation, some regard it as divorce. These examples show that the ruling of divorce is established in times of need, making it the fifth category of a minor's actions. The marginalia explain that the harm is not in the ownership of the right to divorce but in its exercise, and necessity arises from the wife's severe hardships.

Key Terms
طلاق الصبي (talaq al-sabi): Divorce pronounced by a minor (unmarried or prepubescent boy) – valid when necessity demands.
ضرر محض (darar mahd): Pure harm – an action that removes ownership without any countervailing benefit.
ملك الطلاق (milk al-talaq): Ownership of the right to divorce; it is a necessary concomitant of marriage ownership.
حاجة (haja): Need; a situation that would cause harm if not addressed. In this context, the wife's severe hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the minor has reached the age of maturity.
B) Because the harm is in the right to divorce, not in its exercise.
C) Because the right to divorce is a necessary part of marriage and not harmful inherently.
D) Because the guardian always gives permission.

Key Takeaway
A minor's divorce is valid when necessity demands, because the right to divorce is an inseparable part of marriage ownership and is not harmful in itself.

Legal Effects of Minority (Sighar) in Hanafi Usul
Defining minority and its legal effects on obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the legal definition of minority (sighar) in Hanafi usul, explain which obligations are dropped and which are not, and apply the ruling to the case of a minor who kills his testator.

Core Idea
Minority drops what admits pardon, but never drops the obligation of faith.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text defines the legal effect of minority (sighar) on obligations. It distinguishes between obligations that are dropped (due to mercy and lack of full capacity) and those that are not, like faith and rights of people. The key concept is 'uhda (liability) being lifted for what admits 'afw (pardon). The passage also addresses a practical dispute: does a minor lose inheritance if he kills his testator?

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of sighar
Locate the definition: minority is an excuse that drops what admits dropping from the adult of the rights of Allah. Understand that it is due to the intellect not reaching full maturity.
2
Distinguish between dropped and non-dropped obligations
Note that obligations like acts of worship, hudud, and kaffarat are dropped because they admit abrogation and pardon. However, the obligation of faith (iman) is never dropped; a minor's faith is obligatory, not supererogatory. Also, rights of people (like debts, damages) are not dropped.
3
Understand 'uhda and 'afw
The term 'uhda means liability that necessitates accountability. 'Afw means dropping from the adult due to an excuse. Minority lifts 'uhda for what admits 'afw, so the minor is not required to perform but if he does, it counts as an obligatory act.
4
Apply to the case of a minor killing the testator
The text states a minor does not lose inheritance if he kills his testator because that would be a punishment (jaza') and the minor does not deserve punishment due to the lack of 'uhda. This is contrasted with apostasy and slavery, which prevent inheritance due to lack of capacity, not as punishment.
5
Contrast with apostasy and slavery
Unlike killing, apostasy and slavery block inheritance not because of liability but because they negate the capacity to inherit from a free Muslim. The minor who kills still has the capacity; the act is not attributed to him as a blameworthy action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الأهلية
بيان العوارض السماوية
لبقاء صغره، وهو عذر فيسقط به ما يحتمل السقوط عن البالغ من حقوق الله كالعبادات وكالحدود والكفارات، فإنها تحتمل السقوط بالأعذار، وتحتمل النسخ والتبديل في نفسها.
ولا تسقط عنه فرضية الإيمان حتى إذا أدّاه كان فرضًا، فيترتب عليه الأحكام المترتبة على المؤمنين من وقوع الفرقة بينه وبين زوجته المشركة، وحرمان الميراث منها، وجريان الإرث بينه وبين أقاربه المسلمين.
ووضع عنه إلزام الأداء، أي رفع عن الصبي إلزام أداء الإيمان، فلو لم يقرّ في أوان الصبا، أو لم يُعد كلمة الشهادة بعد البلوغ لم يجعل مرتدا.
وجملة الأمر أن توضع عنه العهدة، أي خلص الأمر الكلي في باب الصغر، وحاصل أحكامه أن تسقط عنه عهدة ما يحتمل العفو يعني ما سوى الردّة من العبادات والعقوبات، ويصح منه لو فعله بنفسه من غير عهدة ومطالبة. وله ما لا عهدة فيه أي جاز للصبي ما لا ضرر فيه من قبول الهبة والصدقة ونحوه مما فيه نفع محض.
وهو : أي صغره عذر لعدم بلوغ العقل غاية الاعتدال. (القمر) كالعبادات : من الصلاة والصوم ونحوهما. (القمر) فرضية الإيمان أي وجوب الإيمان؛ لأنه لا يحتمل السقوط بحال (القمر) كان فرضًا أي لا نفلاً، فلا حاجة إلى تجديد أداء الإيمان بعد البلوغ، ولو كان سقطت فرضية الإيمان لكان أداؤه من الصغير نفلاً، وإذ ليس فليس.(القمر) ووضع عنه إلخ أي ليس عليه لزوم الأداء؛ لأنه ليس عقله كافيًا لتوجه الخطاب والتكليف به، فليس عليه وجوب الأداء، لكن إذا أدّاه يقع فرضًا لتحقق نفس الوجوب عليه وهذا كالمسافر ليس عليه وجوب صوم رمضان، وإذا أدّى يقع فرضًا. (القمر) العهدة: أي لزوم ما يوجب المؤاخذة. (القمر) أن تسقط عنه إلخ لأن الصبا من أسباب المرحمة طبعًا وشرعًا. (القمر) العفو أي السقوط عن البالغ بوجه ما. (القمر) ما سوى الردّة إلخ : فإن الردّة لا تحتمل العفو أصلًا. (القمر) ما لا عهدة فيه إلخ لأن الصبا من أسباب المرحمة طبعًا، وشرعًا، أما طبعًا فلأن كل طبع سليم يميل إلى الرحمة على الصغار، وأما شرعًا؛ فلأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام كان يرحم الصغار، فجعل الصّبا سببًا للعفو عن كل عهدة يحتمل العفو مثل الحدود والكفارات وسائر العبادات، بخلاف ما لا يحتمل العفو كالردة وحقوق العباد مثل ضمان المتلفات ونفقة الأقارب. (السنبلي)
ثم قوله فلا يحرم عن الميراث بالقتل عندنا تفريع على قوله : أن توضع عنه العهدة يعني لو قتل الصبي مورثه عمدًا أو خطأ لا يحرم عن ميراثه؛ لأنه عقوبة وعهدة لا يستحقها الصبي. وأورد عليه أنه إذا كان كذلك فلا ينبغي أن يحرم عن الميراث بالكفر والرق؟ فأجاب عنه بقوله : بخلاف الكفر والرق؛ لأن حرمان الميراث بهما ليس من باب الجزاء، بل لعدم الأهلية؛ إذ الكفر والرق ينافي أهلية الميراث من المسلم الحرّ.
Explanation of legal capacity. Explanation of divine impediments. Due to his staying in minority, it is an excuse so what can be dropped from the adult of the rights of Allah is dropped by it, like acts of worship, hudud, and kaffarat, for they admit dropping due to excuses, and they admit abrogation and change in themselves. And the obligation of faith is not dropped from him, so that if he performs it, it is obligatory, and the rulings obligatory upon the believers follow from it: separation between him and his polytheist wife, deprivation of inheritance from her, and inheritance between him and his Muslim relatives. And the obligation of performance is lifted from him, meaning the obligation of performing faith is lifted from the minor, so if he does not affirm during the time of minority, or does not repeat the word of testimony after puberty, he is not considered an apostate. The sum of the matter is that liability is lifted from him, meaning the general command in the chapter of minority is summarized. The summary of its rulings: the liability of what admits pardon is lifted from him, meaning other than apostasy of acts of worship and punishments, and it is valid from him if he does it himself without liability and demand. And he has what has no liability: i.e., permissible for the minor what has no harm in it, such as accepting a gift or charity and similar things which contain pure benefit. It (minority) is an excuse due to the intellect not reaching the extreme of moderation. (al-Qamar) Like acts of worship: prayer, fasting, and their like. (al-Qamar) Obligation of faith: the obligation of faith because it cannot be dropped under any circumstance. (al-Qamar) It is obligatory: i.e., not supererogatory, so there is no need to renew the performance of faith after puberty. If the obligation of faith had been dropped, its performance by the minor would be supererogatory, but it is not. (al-Qamar) 'and the obligation of performance is lifted...' meaning there is no necessity of performance upon him because his intellect is not sufficient for the address and obligation; so there is no obligation of performance on him, but if he performs it, it occurs as obligatory because the very obligation is realized upon him. This is like the traveler: the obligation of fasting Ramadan is not upon him, but if he fasts, it occurs as obligatory. (al-Qamar) al-ʿUhda: obligation that necessitates taking to task. (al-Qamar) That it is dropped from him: because minority is a cause of mercy by nature and by law. (al-Qamar) al-ʿAfw: dropping from the adult in some way. (al-Qamar) Other than apostasy: for apostasy does not admit pardon at all. (al-Qamar) What has no liability: because minority is a cause of mercy by nature and by law; by nature because every sound nature inclines to mercy upon children, and by law because the Prophet (peace be upon him) had mercy on children, so he made minority a cause for pardon from every liability that admits pardon, such as hudud, kaffarat, and all acts of worship, unlike what does not admit pardon, such as apostasy and the rights of people like guarantee of damages and maintenance of relatives. (al-Sunbulī) Then His statement: 'so he is not deprived of inheritance by killing according to us' is a branching from His statement: 'that liability is lifted from him', meaning if the minor kills his testator intentionally or by mistake, he is not deprived of his inheritance, because it is a punishment and liability that the minor does not deserve. And it was objected: if it is so, then he should not be deprived of inheritance by apostasy and slavery? So he answered with His statement: 'Unlike apostasy and slavery', because deprivation of inheritance by them is not from the category of recompense, but due to lack of capacity; for apostasy and slavery contradict the capacity of inheritance from a free Muslim.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn about legal capacity, specifically addressing divine impediments. It opens by stating that minority (sighar) is an excuse that drops certain obligations that are subject to being dropped due to excuses, such as acts of worship, hudud (fixed punishments), and kaffarat (expiation). These obligations admit abrogation and change. Critically, the obligation of faith (iman) is not dropped; if a minor performs faith, it counts as obligatory (fard) not supererogatory (nafl). This means that a minor's acceptance of Islam has full effect: it causes separation from a polytheist spouse, prevents inheritance from the spouse, and establishes inheritance with Muslim relatives. The obligation to specifically perform (ilzam al-ada') is lifted from the minor; if he does not repeat the shahada after puberty, he is not considered an apostate. The key concept is 'uhda (liability) being lifted for obligations that admit 'afw (pardon). These include acts of worship and punishments, but not apostasy or rights of people. The commentator then explains that a minor does not lose inheritance if he kills his testator, because that would be a punishment and liability that the minor cannot bear. In contrast, apostasy and slavery prevent inheritance not as punishment but due to lack of capacity: a slave cannot own property, and a non-Muslim cannot have authority over a Muslim. The passage thus delineates the boundary between what is dropped due to incapacity (minority) and what is not.

Key Terms
الصغر (al-sighar): السن قبل البلوغ، وهو عذر شرعي يسقط ما يحتمل السقوط من التكاليف.
العهدة (al-ʿuhda): لزوم ما يوجب المؤاخذة، أي التبعة التي تستوجب العقوبة أو المطالبة.
العفو (al-ʿafw): السقوط عن البالغ بوجه ما، أي الإسقاط بسبب عذر كالصغر.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a minor (who has not reached puberty) kills his father intentionally?
A) The minor is executed for murder.
B) The minor is deprived of inheritance from his father.
C) The minor inherits from his father normally.
D) The minor inherits only if the father forgave him before death.

Key Takeaway
Minority drops what admits pardon (acts of worship, punishments) but not the obligation of faith or the rights of others, and a minor who kills his testator inherits.

Insanity as a Divine Impediment: Definition and Legal Effect
Defining insanity as a divine impediment and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define insanity according to the Hanafi school and explain its effect on the obligation of acts of worship.

Core Idea
Insanity removes legal capacity, dropping all worship that can be dropped, like prayer.

In this passage, the commentator explains the definition of insanity (junun) as a divine impediment that affects legal capacity (ahliyyah). Understanding this definition and its legal consequences is essential for distinguishing between conditions that remove obligation and those that require compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its opposite
Recognize that insanity is conjoined with minority (sughr) in the discussion of impediments. The opposite of insanity is intellect (aql).
The term is defined in contrast to intellect.
2
Extract the core definition
Insanity is a disease (afah) that settles in the brain, impelling the person to actions contrary to what reason requires, without physical weakness in the limbs.
This distinguishes it from conditions like fainting (ighma') or intoxication where physical weakness may be present.
3
Determine the legal effect
Acts of worship that admit dropping (al-ʿibadat al-muhtamalat li-l-suqut) are dropped from the insane person. Example: prayer (salat) is completely dropped because the insane cannot understand the divine address.
Not all acts are dropped; rights of others (huquq al-ʿibad) are not dropped.
4
Contrast with inheritance impediments
The passage notes that killing, apostasy, and slavery prevent inheritance for different reasons: killing is a punishment, apostasy and slavery due to lack of capacity (ahliyyah) or authority (wilayah).
These distinctions help understand that insanity is a pure excusing factor, not a punitive measure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الجنون] والجنون، عطف على قوله : "الصغر" وهو آفة تحلّ بالدماغ بحيث يبعث على أفعال خلاف مقتضى العقل من غير ضعف في أعضائه وتسقط به العبادات المحتملة للسقوط عنه مثل الصغر لأنه عقوبة إلخ أي لأن حرمان الميراث بالقتل عقوبة إلخ، ولأن موجب القتل يحتمل السقوط بالعفو وبأعذار كثيرة، فيسقط بعذر الصّبا، فكأن مورثه مات حتف أنفه؟ كذا قيل (القمر) كذلك أي إذا كان لا يحرم الصبي الميراث بقتل مورثه (القمر) أن يحرم أي الصبي عن الميراث بالكفر والرق فيرث الصبي الكافر عن المسلم والصبي الرقيق عن الحرّ كما يرث الصبي القاتل عن المقتول.(القمر) بل لعدم الأهلية: فإن الوراثة خلافة الملك وولايته والرق ينافي الملك فينافي الإرث والكفر ينافي أهلية الولاية على المسلم (القمر) ينافي أهلية الميراث إلخ لأن الإرث يقتضي أن يكون الوارث مالكا لما يرثه، والرقيق لا يصح الملك؛ لأن كل ما يملكه الرقيق هو ملك لمولاه، ومثل الرق الكفر في أنه ينافي الإرث؛ لأنه ينافي أهلية الولاية، أي لا ولاية للكافر على المسلم لقوله عزّوجلّ: وَلَنْ يَجْعَلَ اللَّهُ لِلْكَافِرِينَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ سَبِيلاً) (النساء: ١٤١) والإرث مبني على الولاية على ما يشير إليه قوله تعالى : حكاية عن زكريا عليه : وَهَبْ لِي مِنْ لَدُنْكَ وَلِيَا يَرِثُنِي (مريم: ٦،٥) الآية، وعدم الإرث لعدم سببه، أي الولاية فإنه معدوم وجودها في الكافر وعدم أهلية المستحق، فإن الرقيق ليس أهلاً له لا يعد جزاء أي عقوبة (السنبلي) بحيث يبعث : فيختلّ القوة المميزة بين الأمور الحسنة والقبيحة.(القمر) خلاف مقتضى العقل إلخ : قال الشيخ أبو المعين: لا يمكن الوقوف على حقيقة الجنون إلا بعد الوقوف على حقيقة العقل ومحله دافعا له، فالعقل معنى يمكن به الاستدلال من الشاهد على الغائب، والاطلاع على عواقب الأمور والتميز بين الخير والشر، ومحله الدماغ، فالمعنى الموجب لانعدام آثاره وتعطيل أفعاله الباعث للإنسان على أفعال مضادّة لتلك الأفعال من غير ضعف وفتور في الأعضاء يُسمى جنونًا، كذا في "الغاية". (السنبلي) وتسقط به العبادات إلخ كالصلاة لفوات الأهلية بزوال العقل بالجنون فلا يفهم الخطاب. (القمر)
Explanation of insanity. And insanity, conjuncted to His statement: 'minority', is a disease that settles in the brain such that it impels to actions contrary to the requirement of intellect without weakness in its limbs, and by it the acts of worship that admit dropping are dropped from him, like minority. Because it is a punishment etc.: because deprivation of inheritance by killing is a punishment etc., and because the cause of killing admits dropping by pardon and by many excuses, so it drops by the excuse of minority, as if his testator died naturally. Thus it is said. (al-Qamar) Likewise: i.e., if the minor is not deprived of inheritance by killing his testator. (al-Qamar) That he is deprived: i.e., the minor of inheritance by apostasy and slavery, so the minor apostate inherits from a Muslim, and the minor slave inherits from a free person, just as the minor killer inherits from the killed. (al-Qamar) Rather due to lack of capacity: for inheritance is the succession of ownership and authority, and slavery contradicts ownership so it contradicts inheritance, and apostasy contradicts the capacity of authority over a Muslim. (al-Qamar) Contradicts the capacity of inheritance etc. because inheritance requires that the heir be owner of what he inherits, and the slave cannot have valid ownership; because everything the slave owns is the property of his master. And similar to slavery is apostasy in that it contradicts inheritance; because it contradicts the capacity of authority, i.e., no authority for the disbeliever over a Muslim due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And never will Allah make for the disbelievers over the believers a way' (An-Nisa 4:141). And inheritance is based on authority as indicated by His statement, the Exalted, relating from Zakariyya (peace be upon him): 'So grant me from Yourself an inheritor who will inherit me' (Maryam 19:5-6). And the lack of inheritance is due to the lack of its cause, i.e., authority, for its existence is absent in the disbeliever, and the lack of capacity of the recipient, for the slave is not eligible for it. This is not considered recompense i.e., punishment. (al-Sunbulī) 'Such that it impels': the distinguishing power between good and bad things is impaired. (al-Qamar) 'Contrary to the requirement of intellect etc.': Shaykh Abu al-Mu'in said: The reality of insanity cannot be known except after knowing the reality of intellect and its locus as a repeller of it. So intellect is a faculty by which inference from the witnessed to the unseen is possible, and insight into consequences of affairs, and discrimination between good and evil, and its locus is the brain. So the meaning that causes the absence of its effects and the nullification of its actions, which impels a person to actions opposing those actions without weakness and slackness in the limbs, is called insanity. Thus it is in 'al-Ghayah'. (al-Sunbulī) And by it acts of worship that admit dropping are dropped, like prayer, because capacity is lost by the removal of the intellect through insanity, so he does not understand the address. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining insanity (junun) as a divine impediment. It is conjoined with the discussion of minority. The definition: insanity is a disease that afflicts the brain, causing actions contrary to reason without physical weakness. It drops acts of worship that admit dropping, such as prayer, due to loss of capacity (ahliyya) since the insane person cannot understand the divine address. The commentary also reiterates the earlier point about the minor killing the testator: being deprived of inheritance by killing is a punishment, so it drops due to the excuse of minority. In contrast, apostasy and slavery prevent inheritance not as punishment but due to lack of capacity: a slave cannot own property, and a non-Muslim cannot have authority over a Muslim. This is supported by the Quranic verse (4:141) and the story of Zakariyya (19:5-6). The commentator also quotes Shaykh Abu al-Mu'in on the nature of intellect and insanity, emphasizing that insanity is the absence of the intellect's effects.

Key Terms
الجنون (al-junun): آفة تحل بالدماغ تبعث على أفعال خلاف مقتضى العقل بدون ضعف في الأعضاء.
العقل (al-ʿaql): معنى يمكن به الاستدلال من الشاهد على الغائب والتمييز بين الخير والشر، محله الدماغ.
الولاية (al-wilaya): السلطة الشرعية التي تثبت للوارث، والتي ينافيها الكفر فلا ولاية لكافر على مسلم.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the missed prayers of a person who was insane for a period and then recovered?
A) He must make up all missed prayers.
B) He must make up only the prayers he missed during lucid intervals.
C) He does not need to make up any prayers from the period of insanity.
D) His guardian must perform the prayers on his behalf.

Key Takeaway
Insanity is a divine impediment that removes legal capacity, dropping acts of worship that admit complete dropping, but it does not affect other obligations like inheritance rights.
