The Rule: Slavery Negates Property Ownership
Explaining the core rule and the debate on combining ownership and being owned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that slavery prevents ownership of wealth and explain the opposing argument and its rebuttal.

Core Idea
Slavery and ownership are opposites – capability vs incapacity – and cannot combine even from different aspects.

The text opens by establishing a foundational rule in Hanafi usul: slavery (ar-riqq) contradicts ownership of wealth (mālikiyyat al-māl). This is not a mere definition but a legal principle with significant consequences. The author then anticipates and refutes a potential objection: why cannot a slave be considered an owner from the aspect of his humanity while being owned from the aspect of property? The ensuing debate reveals the depth of Hanafi reasoning and the reliance on consensus (ijmā‘) as the strongest proof.

Guided Path
1
State the core rule
Identify the ruling: 'ar-riqq yunāfī mālikiyyat al-māl' – slavery negates ownership of wealth. Understand the reasoning: ownership implies capability (qudra), being owned implies incapacity (ʿajz), and they are opposites.
The rule is stated in absolute terms; no exceptions for mere permission of the master.
2
Examine the objection
A doubt (bahth) is raised: why can these two attributes not combine in a slave from two different aspects? The slave is owned as property but also a human person; perhaps he can own from the human aspect.
This objection represents a hypothetical counterargument, not necessarily a recorded madhhab.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Two responses are given. First, from 'Musir al-Da'ir': capability and incapacity cannot coexist. Second, from Khadim Ahmad: if the slave were considered owner from his humanity, then property (the slave) would own property, which is impossible because the owner (subservient) and the owned (subserved) cannot be the same entity in one state. Ownership of non-wealth is allowed by necessity, but not property.
The argument turns on the logical impossibility of the same entity being both subservient and subserved in the same respect.
4
Identify the preferred proof
The author of al-Tahqīq recommends relying on ijmā‘ (consensus) because the rational proofs are not fully decisive (ad-dalīlu ghayru tāmm). This shifts the basis from pure reason to transmitted consensus.
This shows the Hanafi methodology of preferring transmitted proofs (naql) over rational proofs (ʿaql) when the latter are inconclusive.
5
Apply to the slave's ownership status
Conclude that the slave does not own any property, whether he acquires it or it is given to him. The master owns all his acquisitions.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الأهلية

والرق ينافي مالكية المال لقيام المملوكية فيه حال كونه مالاً، فلا تجتمعان لأن المالكية سمة القدرة والمملوكية سمة العجز. وقيل: فيه بحث؛ لأنه لمَ لا يجوز أن يجتمعا فيه من جهتين مختلفتين، فالمملوكية تكون فيه من جهة المالية والمالكية من جهة الآدمية. حتى لا يملك العبد شيئًا من المال وإن ملكه المولى، فلا تجتمعان لأن المالكية والمملوكية ضدّان. (القمر) فيه بحث: أجاب عنه في "مسير الدائر" بما محصله أن المالكية تنبئ عن القدرة والمملوكية تنبئ عن العجز وهما متنافيان واستحالة اجتماع القدرة والعجز لا يخفى على أحد، فلا يجتمع المالكية والمملوكية. وفيه على ما أقول إن اجتماعهما أيضا من جهتين جائز كما لا يخفى على أحد. وقال البعض أي مولانا خادم أحمد له أجيب بأنه لو قيل لمالكيته من حيث إنه آدمي يلزم منه أن يكون المال مالكًا للمال، وذلك لا يجوز؛ لأن المالك متبذل للمال، والمال متبذل، ولا يجوز أن يكون المتبدِّل متبدلاً في حالة واحدة، بخلاف مالكية ما ليس بمال؛ لأن الضرورة داعية إلى إثباتها، كذا في شروح الحسامي، فافهم. وفيه أنه يجوز أن يكون المتبدّل متبدّلاً في حالة واحدة من جهتين، ولنعم ما قال صاحب "التحقيق": إن الأولى أن يتمسك في هذا الحكم بالإجماع فإن الدليل غير تام.

حتى لا يملك العبد والمكاتب التسرّي، أي الأخذ بالسّرية، وهي الأمة التي بوأتها وأعددتها للوطء وإن أذن لهما المولى بذلك. وإنما حُص المكاتب بالذكر مع أن المدبّر أيضا كذلك؛ لأنه صار أحق بمكاسبه يدًا، فيُوهم ذلك جواز التسرّي، فأزال الوهم بذكره.

ولا تصح منهما حجّة الإسلام حتى لو حجّا يقع نفلا وإن كان بإذن المولى؛ لأن منافعهما فيما سوى الصلاة والصيام تبقى للمولى ولا تكون لهما قدرة على أدائه، بخلاف الفقير إذا حجّ، ثم استغنى حيث يقع ما أدّى عن الفرض؛ لأن ملك المال ليس بشرط لذاته، وإنما شرط للتمكّن عن الأداء.

ولا ينافي مالكية غير المال كالنكاح والدم فإنه مالك للنكاح؛ لأن قضاء شهوة الفرج فرض ولا سبيل له إلى التسري، فتعيّن النكاح، ولكنه موقوف على رضاء المولى؛ لأن المهر يتعلّق برقبته فيباع فيه، وفي ذلك إضرار للمولى، فلا بد من رضائه. وكذا هو مالك لدمه؛ لأنه محتاج إلى البقاء، ولا بقاء إلا به؛ ولهذا لا يملك المولى إتلاف دمه. وصح إقرار العبد بالقصاص؛ لأنه في ذلك مثل الحرّ.

وينافي كمال الحال في أهلية الكرامات الموضوعة للبشر كالذمة. فإن كمال الحال بالشرف والرقية ذلّ فلا يجتمعان. الموضوعة للبشر: أي في الدنيا، وأما الكرامات الأخروية فبناؤها على التقوى، والحر والعبد فيه يتساويان.
Explanation of legal capacity. Slavery prevents ownership of wealth because of the presence of being owned in it while it is wealth, so they do not combine because ownership is a mark of capability and being owned is a mark of incapacity. It was said: There is investigation in this; because why is it not permissible for them to combine in it from two different aspects? Being owned is from the aspect of property, and ownership is from the aspect of humanity. So the slave does not own any wealth even if the master owns him, they do not combine because ownership and being owned are opposites. There is investigation: it was answered in 'Musir al-Da'ir' with the summary that ownership indicates capability and being owned indicates incapacity, and they are contradictory, and the impossibility of combining capability and incapacity is not hidden from anyone, so ownership and being owned do not combine. According to what I say, their combination from two aspects is permissible as is not hidden from anyone. And some, i.e. our master Khadim Ahmad, answered that if it were said that he owns from the aspect of being human, it would necessitate that wealth owns wealth, and that is not permissible because the owner is subservient to wealth and wealth is subservient, and it is not permissible that the subservient be subservient in a single state, unlike ownership of non-wealth because necessity calls for its establishment, as in the commentaries of al-Hisami, so understand. And it is that it is permissible that the subservient be subservient in a single state from two aspects. How excellent is what the author of al-Tahqiq said: It is better to adhere in this ruling to consensus for the proof is not complete. So the slave and the mukatib do not own concubinage, i.e. taking a slave woman as a concubine, even if the master permits it. The mukatib is specifically mentioned although the mudabbar is also like that because he became more entitled to his earnings by hand, so that might suggest permissibility of concubinage, so he removed the doubt by mentioning it. And the Hajj of Islam is not valid from them so that if they perform Hajj it falls as nafl even with the master's permission, because their benefits other than prayer and fasting remain for the master and they do not have the ability to perform it, unlike the poor person who performs Hajj then becomes wealthy, where what he performed fulfills the obligation, because ownership of wealth is not a condition for itself, but rather is a condition for ability to perform. And ownership of non-wealth like marriage and blood does not prevent it, for he owns marriage because fulfilling sexual desire is an obligation and he has no way to concubinage, so marriage is determined, but it is dependent on the master's consent because the dowry is attached to his neck so he is sold for it, and that harms the master, so his consent is necessary. Likewise he owns his blood because he needs survival, and there is no survival except with it; for this the master does not own destroying his blood. And the slave's acknowledgment of retaliation is valid because in that he is like a free person. And it prevents perfection of state in the capacity of honors placed for humans like legal capacity. For perfection of state is with honor and slavery is humiliation, so they do not combine. 'Placed for humans' means in this world, while eschatological honors are based on piety, and the free person and the slave are equal in that.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the legal effects of slavery (رق) on capacity (أهلية). The author begins with a core rule: slavery contradicts ownership of wealth. He explains that ownership requires capacity (قدرة) while being owned (مملوكية) implies incapacity (عجز), so they cannot coexist. A doubt is raised: why not combine from two aspects? The slave is owned as property but a person as human. The commentator answers via various scholars: if a slave were considered owner from his humanity, then property would own property, which is impossible because the owner is subservient to property. However, ownership of non-wealth (like marriage) is allowed by necessity. The author of al-Tahqiq prefers to rely on consensus for this ruling. Next, specific applications: a slave cannot take a concubine (تسرّي) even if master permits, and the mukatib is mentioned to avoid misunderstanding. Also, a slave's Hajj does not fulfill the obligation because his benefits belong to the master; unlike a poor free person who later becomes rich. However, a slave does own the right to marry and to his own blood (دم) because of necessity – marriage for lawful fulfillment of desire, and blood for survival. Hence the master cannot kill him, and the slave's confession in retaliation cases is valid. Finally, slavery negates perfect worldly honors (كرامات) like full legal capacity (ذمة), but religious honors are based on piety where slave and free are equal.

Key Terms
الرق (ar-riqq): Slavery, a legal status that negates ownership of wealth and full worldly legal capacity.
المالكية (al-mālikiyya): The capacity to own and dispose of property.
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary, what is the preferred basis for the ruling that a slave cannot own property?
A) Rational proof that capability and incapacity cannot combine
B) The argument that property would own property
C) Consensus (ijmā‘) because rational proof is not complete
D) The master's absolute ownership

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that slavery negates property ownership (al-riqq yunāfī mālikiyyat al-māl) is established on both rational and transmitted proofs, with consensus as the strongest support.

Applications: Specific Rights and Acts of a Slave
Detailing the effects of slavery on specific legal capacities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that slavery negates property ownership but allows certain non-property rights to explain the specific rulings on concubinage, hajj, marriage, blood, and worldly honors.

Core Idea
Slavery blocks property ownership but necessity allows marriage and blood-rights; religious honors are equal.

After establishing the core rule that slavery negates property ownership, the text applies this rule to several specific cases: concubinage (tasarrī), the Hajj obligation, marriage rights, blood-money (dam), and the slave's legal capacity. Each application demonstrates how the general principle interacts with other legal considerations, such as necessity (darūra) and the distinction between worldly and otherworldly honors.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the case of concubinage (tasarrī)
The text rules that a slave (and a mukātab) cannot take a slave-concubine, even with the master's permission. Understand why: since the slave cannot own property, he cannot acquire a concubine (who is a form of property). The mukātab is mentioned explicitly to remove any doubt that he might own his earnings.
The mudabbar (slave promised freedom) is not mentioned but is similar.
2
Understand the Hajj ruling
A slave's Hajj does not fulfill the obligatory Islamic Hajj (Ḥajjat al-Islām), even if the master permits. It counts as nafl. Reason: the slave's benefits (manāfiʿ) belong to the master, so he lacks the required capability (qudra) to perform the obligation. Contrast with a poor free person who later becomes wealthy – his Hajj suffices because his lack of wealth was temporary and not a status.
This shows that the condition for Hajj is not mere ownership of wealth but actual ability to use it, which the slave lacks.
3
Examine marriage and blood rights
Despite the ban on property ownership, a slave can marry and owns his blood (i.e., cannot be killed by the master). Reasoning: necessity (darūra) – marriage is needed for lawful sexual fulfillment, and blood-ownership is needed for survival. Foreign marriage is dependent on the master's consent because dowry is attached to the slave's neck (i.e., may be sold to pay it).
This is an exception to the general rule, permitted by necessity.
4
Apply the rule to worldly honors
Slavery negates full worldly honor (karāmāt), such as having a complete legal personality (dhimma). The slave's status is one of humiliation (dhull), which contradicts honor (sharaf). However, eschatological honors are based on piety (taqwā), where slave and free are equal.
This distinction explains why a slave cannot be a witness in certain cases? Not explicitly, but it highlights the limited capacity.

Source Text

(القمر) فيه بحث: أجاب عنه في "مسير الدائر" بما محصله أن المالكية تنبئ عن القدرة والمملوكية تنبئ عن العجز وهما متنافيان واستحالة اجتماع القدرة والعجز لا يخفى على أحد، فلا يجتمع المالكية والمملوكية. وفيه على ما أقول إن اجتماعهما أيضا من جهتين جائز كما لا يخفى على أحد. وقال البعض أي مولانا خادم أحمد له أجيب بأنه لو قيل لمالكيته من حيث إنه آدمي يلزم منه أن يكون المال مالكًا للمال، وذلك لا يجوز؛ لأن المالك متبذل للمال، والمال متبذل، ولا يجوز أن يكون المتبدِّل متبدلاً في حالة واحدة، بخلاف مالكية ما ليس بمال؛ لأن الضرورة داعية إلى إثباتها، كذا في شروح الحسامي، فافهم. وفيه أنه يجوز أن يكون المتبدّل متبدّلاً في حالة واحدة من جهتين، ولنعم ما قال صاحب "التحقيق": إن الأولى أن يتمسك في هذا الحكم بالإجماع فإن الدليل غير تام.

حتى لا يملك العبد والمكاتب التسرّي، أي الأخذ بالسّرية، وهي الأمة التي بوأتها وأعددتها للوطء وإن أذن لهما المولى بذلك. وإنما حُص المكاتب بالذكر مع أن المدبّر أيضا كذلك؛ لأنه صار أحق بمكاسبه يدًا، فيُوهم ذلك جواز التسرّي، فأزال الوهم بذكره.

ولا تصح منهما حجّة الإسلام حتى لو حجّا يقع نفلا وإن كان بإذن المولى؛ لأن منافعهما فيما سوى الصلاة والصيام تبقى للمولى ولا تكون لهما قدرة على أدائه، بخلاف الفقير إذا حجّ، ثم استغنى حيث يقع ما أدّى عن الفرض؛ لأن ملك المال ليس بشرط لذاته، وإنما شرط للتمكّن عن الأداء.

ولا ينافي مالكية غير المال كالنكاح والدم فإنه مالك للنكاح؛ لأن قضاء شهوة الفرج فرض ولا سبيل له إلى التسري، فتعيّن النكاح، ولكنه موقوف على رضاء المولى؛ لأن المهر يتعلّق برقبته فيباع فيه، وفي ذلك إضرار للمولى، فلا بد من رضائه. وكذا هو مالك لدمه؛ لأنه محتاج إلى البقاء، ولا بقاء إلا به؛ ولهذا لا يملك المولى إتلاف دمه. وصح إقرار العبد بالقصاص؛ لأنه في ذلك مثل الحرّ.

وينافي كمال الحال في أهلية الكرامات الموضوعة للبشر كالذمة. فإن كمال الحال بالشرف والرقية ذلّ فلا يجتمعان. الموضوعة للبشر: أي في الدنيا، وأما الكرامات الأخروية فبناؤها على التقوى، والحر والعبد فيه يتساويان.
Explanation of legal capacity. Slavery prevents ownership of wealth because of the presence of being owned in it while it is wealth, so they do not combine because ownership is a mark of capability and being owned is a mark of incapacity. It was said: There is investigation in this; because why is it not permissible for them to combine in it from two different aspects? 'Placed for humans' means in this world, while eschatological honors are based on piety, and the free person and the slave are equal in that.

التسرّي (al-tasarrī): Taking a female slave as a concubine, which requires ownership of the slave-woman.
حج الإسلام (ḥajjat al-islām): The obligatory Hajj, a pillar of Islam that is required once in a lifetime for those who have the ability (istitāʿa).

The Two Layers of Inviolability (Ismah)
Definition of al-ismah and its two types; proof from Quran 4:93

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-ismah, distinguish its two types (mu'aththimah and muqawwamah), and state that a slave's blood is as inviolable as a free person's according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A slave's blood is as sacred as a free person's – inviolability has two layers: sin-based and compensation-based.

This passage from Noor al-Anwaar clarifies a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul: that slavery does not diminish the sanctity of life. The author, Imam Abu Bakr al-Sherbilali, explains that every believer's blood is inviolable by virtue of faith alone, and an additional layer of protection requiring financial compensation is established by residence in Dar al-Islam. Both layers apply equally to slave and free. The Quranic verse (4:93) serves as the primary proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of 'ismah
The passage introduces two distinct categories: mu'aththimah (sin-based) and muqawwamah (compensation-based). Note that mu'aththimah focuses on sin and expiation, while muqawwamah adds financial liability.
These terms are foundational in Hanafi usul for discussions on protection of life.
2
Determine the basis of each type
Mu'aththimah is rooted in iman (faith) alone – any believer, regardless of status, has this protection. Muqawwamah is rooted in dar (abode) – being in Dar al-Islam triggers compensation liability.
A believer in Dar al-Harb without protection only has mu'aththimah.
3
Note the equality of slave and free
The passage explicitly states 'al-'abdu fīhi ka al-hurri' – the slave is like the free in both protections. Thus, killing a slave believer incurs sin and kaffarah, and if in Dar al-Islam, also qisas/diyah.
This contradicts any assumption that a slave's life is less protected.
4
Apply the Quranic proof
The verse 'and whoever kills a believer intentionally, his reward is Hell' (4:93) is cited. The word 'mu'min' (believer) is general, including free and slave, proving the sin-based protection for any believer.
The verse proves mu'aththimah; muqawwamah is derived from other sources or istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والمقومة بداره، أي العصمة التي توجب القيمة تثبت بدار الإيمان، فمن قتل من المسلمين في دار الإسلام تجب الدية والقصاص على قاتله بخلاف من أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر إلى دار الإسلام، فإنه لا يجب على قاتله إلا الكفارة دون الدية والقصاص؛ إذ ليس له إلا العصمة المؤثمة دون المقومة.

(القمر) المؤثمة إلخ اعلم أن العصمة عبارة عن حرمة التعرض بالإتلاف في صاحب الشرع وصاحب الدم، فهي على نوعين: مؤلمة توجب الإثم فقط على تقدير التعرض وهي تثبت بالإيمان فقط، ومقوّمة توجب مع الإثم القصاص أو الدية، وهي تثبت بالدار أي بالإحراز بدار الإسلام والعبد يساوي الحر في الأمرين فيساويه في العظمتين. (السنبلي)
يستحق الإثم إلخ كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِناً مُتَعَمِّداً فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ﴾ (النساء: ٩٣) (القمر) والمقومة: أي الموجبة للضمان، وهو القيمة على تقدير التعرض، وهذا معطوف على المؤثمة. (القمر) إذ ليس له أي لذلك المسلم الغير المهاجر. (القمر)
And that, i.e. slavery, does not affect the inviolability of blood, i.e., it does not remove the inviolability of blood. Rather his blood is protected just as the blood of the free person is protected; because the protective inviolability that entails sin is by faith, meaning one who is a believer, his killer incurs sin, so expiation is obligatory upon him. And the compensatory protection is by his abode, i.e., the inviolability that necessitates value is established by the abode of faith. So whoever kills a Muslim in the abode of Islam, diyah and qisas are obligatory upon the killer, unlike one who becomes Muslim in the abode of war and did not migrate to the abode of Islam, for upon his killer only expiation is obligatory, not diyah or qisas; because he only has the sin-entailing protection, not the compensatory one. And the slave is equal to the free person in each of the two protections. As for faith, it is apparent. And the slave may be of a higher rank than his master...
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the effect of slavery on the inviolability of blood (ismat al-dam). It distinguishes two types: (1) mu'aththimah, which makes killing sinful and requires kaffarah, based solely on faith; (2) muqawwamah, which also imposes financial compensation (diyah/qisas), based on being in Dar al-Islam. A slave is equal to a free person in both, so killing a slave is a major sin and requires expiation, and if in Dar al-Islam, also diyah/qisas. The Quranic verse (4:93) is cited as proof for the sin-entailing protection.

Key Terms
العصمة (al-ismah): Inviolability; protection of life from being lawfully taken
المؤثمة (al-mu'aththimah): Protection that makes killing sinful, based on faith alone
المقومة (al-muqawwamah): Protection that imposes financial compensation (diyah/qisas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Residence in Dar al-Islam
B) Faith (iman)
C) Free status
D) Slavery

Key Takeaway
A slave's blood is as protected as a free person's; inviolability has two layers: sin-based (from faith) and compensation-based (from abode in Dar al-Islam).

Intra-Madhhab Ikhtilaf: The Restricted Slave's Confession to Theft
Dispute on a restricted slave's confession to theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on a restricted slave's confession to theft and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of each.

Core Idea
The confession of a restricted slave to theft triggers a three-way split among the Hanafi imams on amputation and financial liability.

In this passage, we encounter a dispute among the three great Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—on the consequences of a restricted slave's confession to theft. While all agree on the basic rule for permitted slaves, the restriction (hajr) introduces complexity regarding the master's rights and the victim's claim. This lesson unpacks each position and the practical differences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish slave categories
Identify the difference between a permitted slave (al-ma'dhun) and a restricted slave (al-mahjur) and recall the basic ruling for a permitted slave's confession.
Permitted slaves have full capacity for confessions; restricted slaves do not, leading to ikhtilaf.
2
Identify the scenarios
Note the two sub-scenarios: property consumed vs. property existing, and within the latter, master confirms vs. master denies.
The passage gives agreed rulings for property consumed and master confirmed cases; the dispute is only when property exists and master denies.
3
State each imam's ruling
For the disputed scenario (property exists, master denies), record the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding amputation and property return.
Write them side by side for comparison.
4
Analyze thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, both hadd and property return; under Abu Yusuf, hadd but no return (value guaranteed after emancipation); under Muhammad, no hadd and no return (only guarantee after emancipation).
This shows how each ruling affects the victim and the master.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيجب القطع في المستهلكة ولا ضمان عليه؛ لأنه لا يجتمع مع القطع، ويردّ المال في القائمة إلى المسروق منه ويقطع، وهذا كله في المأذون. وفي المحجور اختلاف، أي إن أقرّ العبد المحجور بالسرقة، فإن كان المال هالكًا قطع ولا ضمان، وإن كان قائما فإن صدقه المولى قُطع ويردّ ، وإن كذبه المولى ففيه اختلاف، أي المال فعند أبي حنيفة لله يُقطع ويردّ ، وعند أبي يوسف له يقطع ولا يردّ، ولكن يضمن أي المال على المسروق منه مثله بعد الإعتاق، وعند محمد حلبيه لا يقطع ولا يردّ ، بل يضمن المال بعد الإعتاق. ودلائل الكل في كتب الفقه.
So amputation is obligatory for consumed property and no guarantee upon him; because it does not combine with amputation. And the property that still exists is returned to the one stolen from, and he is amputated. All this is for the permitted slave. And regarding the restricted slave, there is disagreement: if the restricted slave confesses to theft, then if the property is destroyed, he is amputated and no guarantee. If it exists, then if the master confirms him, he is amputated and the property is returned. If the master denies him, then there is disagreement regarding the property: according to Abu Hanifa, he is amputated and the property is returned; according to Abu Yusuf, he is amputated and the property is not returned, but he guarantees the equivalent after emancipation; according to Muhammad, he is not amputated and the property is not returned, but he guarantees the property after emancipation. And the proofs of all are in the books of fiqh.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a fiqh ruling on the confession of a restricted slave (محجور) to theft, along with the disagreement among the three major Hanafi imams. The ruling distinguishes between a permitted slave (مأذون) and a restricted one. For the permitted slave, the ruling is straightforward: if the stolen property is consumed (مستهلكة), the slave is amputated and does not guarantee the value, because the hadd (amputation) and financial liability cannot combine. If the property still exists (قائمة), it is returned to the victim and the slave is amputated. For a restricted slave (e.g., one under interdiction due to bankruptcy or other reasons), the issue is more complex. If the property is destroyed, the slave is amputated without liability. If it remains, the master's confirmation determines the outcome: if the master confirms the theft, the slave is amputated and the property returned. If the master denies, the imams differ: Abu Hanifa holds that both amputation and return are carried out, as the confession is effective for hadd and the property is considered stolen from the victim. Abu Yusuf agrees on amputation but refuses to return the property to the victim because the slave's confession regarding property is a confession against the master, who denies it; instead, the slave guarantees the equivalent after emancipation. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that neither amputation nor return occurs, because the confession against the master is invalid; the slave only guarantees the value after emancipation. The text concludes that the full proofs for each position are found in the fiqh books.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma’dhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade
المحجور (al-maḥjūr): A slave restricted from financial transactions due to interdiction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when a restricted slave confesses to theft and the stolen property exists but the master denies, what is the ruling?
A) The slave is amputated but the property is not returned to the victim.
B) The slave is not amputated and the property is not returned.
C) The slave is amputated and the property is returned to the victim.
D) The slave guarantees the equivalent of the property after emancipation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains internal ikhtilaf on the effect of a restricted slave's confession, illustrating that even within a madhhab, multiple valid rulings exist based on different interpretive approaches.

Illness and Its Legal Effects: Capacity, Worship, and Interdiction
Definition of illness and its dual effect on ahliyyah and financial transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how illness affects legal capacity according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between transactions that remain valid and those subject to rescission upon death from that illness.

Core Idea
Illness does not negate legal capacity but triggers partial interdiction only if it leads to death, retroactive to its onset.

This passage defines illness in the context of legal capacity (ahliyyah) and explains its nuanced legal effects. While illness does not remove a person's capacity for obligation or expression, it imposes certain restrictions on financial transactions to protect the rights of creditors and heirs. Understanding this balance is essential for deriving correct rulings in matters of worship and property during illness.

Guided Path
1
Define illness and its legal status
Identify the text's definition of illness as a bodily state that disrupts natural balance. Note that it does not negate legal capacity (ahliyyah).
The definition is explicit: 'حالة للبدن يزول بها اعتدال الطبيعة'.
2
Understand the effect on ahliyyah
Recognize that illness preserves both ahliyyat al-hukm (capacity for obligation) and ahliyyat al-ibara (capacity for expression). Marriage, divorce, and other verbal statements remain valid.
The text says: 'لا ينافي أهلية الحكم والعبارة'.
3
Identify the conditions for interdiction (hajr)
Determine that interdiction applies only when the illness is fatal and actually leads to death ('متصل بالموت'). The interdiction is retroactive to the onset of the illness.
The cause of interdiction is the fatal illness, not mere illness.
4
Analyze which transactions are affected
Distinguish between original needs (e.g., marriage with proper dower) – which are valid despite interdiction – and transactions that admit cancellation (e.g., gifts, below-market sales). The latter are initially valid but rescinded upon death to protect heirs/creditors.
The text mentions 'الحوائج الأصلية' as exempt from interdiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرض، عطف على ما قبله وهو حالة للبدن يزول بها اعتدال الطبيعة، وأنه لا ينافي أهلية الحكم والعبارة، أي يكون أهلاً لوجوب الحكم وللتعبير عن المقاصد بالعبارة حتى صحّ نكاحه وطلاقه وسائر ما يتعلق بعبارته، ولكنه لما كان سبب الموت، وأنه عجز خالص كان المرض من أسباب العجز، فشرعت العبادات عليه بالقدرة الممكنة، فيصلّي قاعدًا إن لم يقدر على القيام ومستلقيا إن لم يقدر على القعود. ولما كان الموت علّة الخلافة، أي خلافة الوارث والغرماء في ماله كان المرض من أسباب تعلّق حق الوارث والغريم بماله، فيكون من أسباب الحجر بقدر ما يتعلّق به صيانة الحق، أي حق الغريم والوارث، ويكون المريض محجورًاً من قدر الدين الذي هو حق الغريم، ومن الثلثين الذي هو حق الوارث، ولكن لا مطلقا، بل إذا اتصل بالموت، ويموت من ذلك المرض، فحينئذ يظهر كونه محجورًا، ولكن يكون مستندًا إلى أوّله أي يقال عند الموت: إنه محجور عن التصرّف من أول المرض، حتى لا يؤثر المرض، متعلّق بقوله: بقدر ما يتعلّق به صيانة الحق أي إنما يؤثر المرض فيما تعلّق به حق الغير، ولا يؤثر فيما لا يتعلّق به حق غريم ووارث کالنكاح بمهر المثل، فإنه من الحوائج الأصلية، وحقهم يتعلق فيما يفضل منها، فيصح في الحال لهذا الحجر لبقاء النسل بالنكاح الورثة والغرماء الحوائج الأصلية كل تصرف يحتمل الفسخ كالهبة والمحاباة، وهو البيع بأقل من القيمة؛ إذ الموت مشكوك في الحال، وليس في صحة هذا التصرّف في الحال ضرر بأحد، فينبغي أن يصح حينئذ. ينتقض إن احتيج إليه أي إلى النقص عند تحقق الحاجة. اتصل بالموت لأن علة الحجر مرض مميت لا نفس المرض. (القمر) اتصل بالموت لأنه لا يظهر أن هذا مرض الموت إلا باتصاله بالموت فإذا اتصل به ثبت أنه مرض الموت فيثبت الحجر مستندا إلى أوّله؛ لأن سبب الحجر المرض المميت، فيضاف الحجر إلى جميع السبب من يوم ابتداء إلى يوم الموت.(السنبلي) ضرر بأحد : لأنه قابل الفسخ إذا احتيج إليه حتى يصح هبة المريض و وصيته في جميع ماله في الحال؛ لأنه لا يلحق الضرر بأحد في الحال، وإنما يلحق بالموت، فإذا مات المريض من ذلك المرض يفسخ هبة ووصية بقدر ما يقع به صيانة الحق؛ لأنه حينئذ احتيج إلى فسخه صيانة لحق الغريم والوارث. (السنبلي) إن احتيج إليه بأن كان الموهوب والمحابي في حق الغريم.(القمر)
And illness – connected to what preceded – is a state of the body by which the balance of nature ceases. And it does not negate legal capacity for obligation and expression, meaning he is qualified for obligation of rulings and for expressing intentions by speech, so that his marriage, divorce, and all that pertains to his speech are valid. But because it is a cause of death, and it is pure incapacity, illness is among the causes of incapacity. So acts of worship are prescribed upon him according to possible ability: he prays sitting if unable to stand, and lying on his side if unable to sit. And since death is the cause of succession – i.e., succession of the heir and creditors in his property – illness is among the causes of attachment of the right of the heir and creditor to his property. Thus it is among causes of interdiction to the extent of safeguarding the right – i.e., the right of the creditor and heir. The sick person is interdicted from the amount of debt which is the right of the creditor, and from two-thirds which is the right of the heir. But not absolutely, rather if it is connected to death and he dies from that illness, then at that time his being interdicted appears, but it is based from its beginning – meaning it is said at death: he has been interdicted from disposition from the beginning of the illness, so that the illness affects only what is attached to the right of others. It does not affect what no right of creditor or heir is attached to, like marriage with proper dower, for it is among original needs, and their right attaches to what exceeds it, so it is valid at the time of this interdiction – to preserve progeny through marriage – [for] heirs and creditors. Original needs: every disposition that admits cancellation like gift and undervalue sale – i.e., selling for less than value – because death is uncertain at the time, and in the validity of such disposition at the time there is no harm to anyone, so it should be valid then. It is rescinded if needed – i.e., the deficiency when need is realized. Connected to death because the cause of interdiction is fatal illness, not illness itself. (al-Qamar) Connected to death because it is not apparent that this is a deathly illness except by its connection to death; so when it is connected, it is established that it is a deathly illness, thus interdiction is established based from its beginning, because the cause of interdiction is fatal illness, so interdiction is added to the entire cause from the day it began to the day of death. (al-Sunbuli) Harm to anyone: because it admits cancellation if needed, so that the gift and will of the sick person in all his property are valid at the time, because no harm befalls anyone at the time, rather it befalls at death. So if the sick person dies from that illness, the gift and will are rescinded to the extent that safeguards the right – because then it is needed to rescind them to protect the right of the creditor and heir. (al-Sunbuli) If needed – meaning that the gifted and undervalued property is in respect of the creditor’s right. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage defines illness (مرض) in the context of legal capacity (أهلية) and explains its nuanced effects on a person's legal actions. It begins by defining illness as a bodily state that disrupts natural balance. Importantly, illness does not negate a person's capacity for legal obligation (أهلية الحكم) or expressive capacity (أهلية العبارة). Thus, the sick person's marriage, divorce, and other verbal statements remain valid. However, because illness often leads to death and signifies pure incapacity (عجز خالص), it triggers certain legal consequences. First, regarding worship (عبادات), the sick person is obliged to perform acts to the best of their ability, e.g., praying sitting if unable to stand. Second, regarding financial transactions, since death is the cause of succession (خلافة) for heirs and creditors, illness becomes a reason for the attachment of their rights to the patient's property. Consequently, the sick person is deemed interdicted (محجور) to the extent of debts (حق الغريم) and two-thirds of the property (حق الوارث). This interdiction, however, is not absolute; it only applies if the illness is connected to death (متصل بالموت) – i.e., the person dies from that illness. In that case, the interdiction is retroactive to the onset of the illness. The passage clarifies that the interdiction only affects transactions that involve the rights of others (الغير). Transactions that are 'original needs' (حوائج أصلية) like marriage with proper dower (مهر المثل) are valid because they preserve progeny and do not harm heirs/creditors (any excess belongs to them). Other transactions that admit cancellation (يحتمل الفسخ) like gifts (هبة) or sales below market value (محاباة) are initially valid because at the time of transaction, death is uncertain and there is no immediate harm. However, if the patient dies from that illness, such transactions are rescinded to the extent needed to protect the rights of creditors and heirs. The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sunbuli reinforces these points.

Key Terms
أهلية الحكم والعبارة (ahliyyat al-ḥukm wa al-ʿibāra): Capacity to be subject to legal obligations and to express one's will through speech
الحجر (al-ḥajr): Interdiction preventing a person from freely disposing of property to protect others' rights
الحوائج الأصلية (al-ḥawā’ij al-aṣliyya): Essential needs like marriage and basic transactions

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) From the moment the person falls ill.
B) From when the illness is diagnosed as terminal.
C) From the time the person dies from that illness, retroactive to its onset.
D) From the moment the patient makes a gift.

Key Takeaway
Illness preserves legal capacity but imposes partial interdiction on financial transactions only when the illness is fatal and connected to death, with retroactive effect.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Legal Rule of Interdiction in Terminal Illness (Marad al-Mawt)
Explaining the Hanafi rule of interdiction in terminal illness via causal analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of interdiction (hajar) for the terminally ill, identify the condition of ittisal bi al-mawt (connection to death), and apply the distinction between dispositions that admit rescission (ghayr lazim) and those that do not (lazim).

Core Idea
Interdiction in terminal illness only takes effect retroactively upon death, protecting creditors and heirs but not blocking essential needs.

This passage from Noor al-Anwaar explains a key usul al-fiqh rule: the legal capacity (ahliyya) of a person in marad al-mawt is restricted not immediately but conditionally upon death from that illness. The restriction is based on the illa (cause) that death transfers property to heirs and creditors, so a fatal illness triggers a similar attachment of rights. Understanding this rule is essential for distinguishing between valid and invalid transactions of a terminally ill patient in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling on Utterances
The passage begins by stating that a terminally ill person's marriage, divorce, and other utterances (aqwal) are valid because these depend on sound speech, not physical ability. This establishes that the ill person retains full capacity for verbal acts.
This is the baseline: the ill person is not incapacitated in speech.
2
Locate the Illa (Cause) for Interdiction
The text states: 'wa lamma kana al-mawt ‘illat al-khilafah ... kana al-marad min asbab ta‘alluq haqq al-warith wa al-gharim bi malih'. Death is the cause of succession; illness is a cause of attachment of the rights of heirs and creditors to his property. This causal link is the foundation for the rule of interdiction.
The illa is not the illness itself, but its connection to death as a cause of property transfer.
3
Identify the Condition (Qayd) for Interdiction
The interdiction (hajar) only takes effect when the illness is connected to death (ittasala bi al-mawt) and the person dies from it. It is not immediate; it retroactively applies from the beginning of the illness upon death. The text says: 'la yuzhar anna hadha marad al-mawt illa bi ittisalihi bi al-mawt'.
This condition distinguishes terminal illness from simple illness.
4
Classify Dispositions into Two Categories
Dispositions are divided into: (1) those that can be rescinded (yahmal al-faskh) like gifts (hiba) and sales below value (muhaba), which are valid initially but can be rescinded after death to protect claimants; and (2) those that cannot be rescinded (la yahmal al-faskh) like manumission (‘itq), which are treated as suspended on death (mu‘allaq bi al-mawt) – the slave becomes free only after death and must pay his value.
The key distinction: rescindable acts are valid now and rescinded later; non-rescindable acts are delayed until death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الأهلية
بيان المرض
حتى صح نكاحه وطلاقه وسائر ما يتعلق بعبارته، ولكنه لما كان سبب الموت، وأنه، أي المريض، أي المرض، أي والحال أن الموت عجز خالص كان المرض من أسباب العجز، فشرعت العبادات عليه، أي المريض، بالقدرة الممكنة، فيصلي قاعدًا إن لم يقدر على القيام ومستلقيا إن لم يقدر على القعود.
ولما كان الموت علة الخلافة، أي خلافة الوارث والغرماء في ماله كان المرض من أسباب تعلق، الميت، حق الوارث والغريم بماله، فيكون من أسباب الحجر بقدر ما يتعلق به صيانة الحق، أي المريض، أي على المريض، حق الغريم والوارث، ويكون المريض محجورًا من قدر الدين الذي هو حق الغريم، ومن الثلثين الذي هو حق الوارث، ولكن لا مطلقا، بل إذا اتصل بالموت، ويموت من ذلك المرض، فحينئذ يظهر كونه محجورًا، ولكن يكون مستندًا إلى أوله أي يقال عند الموت: إنه محجور عن التصرف من أول المرض، حتى لا يؤثر المرض، متعلق بقوله: "بقدر ما يتعلق به صيانة الحق" أي إنما يؤثر المرض فيما تعلق به حق الغير، ولا يؤثر فيما لا يتعلق به حق غريم ووارث كالنكاح بمهر المثل، فإنه من الحوائج الأصلية، وحقهم يتعلق فيما يفضل منها، فيصح في الحال، أي هذا الحجر، لبقاء النسل بالنكاح الورثة والغرماء، الحوائج الأصلية، كل تصرف يحتمل الفسخ كالهبة والمحاباة، وهو البيع بأقل من القيمة؛ إذ الموت مشكوك في الحال، وليس في صحة هذا التصرف في الحال ضرر بأحد، فينبغي أن يصح حينئذ.
ينتقض إن احتيج إليه أي إلى النقص عند تحقق الحاجة.
اتصل بالموت لأن علة الحجر مرض مميت لا نفس المرض. (القمر) اتصل بالموت لأنه لا يظهر أن هذا مرض الموت إلا باتصاله بالموت فإذا اتصل به ثبت أنه مرض الموت فيثبت الحجر مستندا إلى أوله؛ لأن سبب الحجر المرض المميت، فيضاف الحجر إلى جميع السبب من يوم ابتداء إلى يوم الموت.(السنبلي) ضرر بأحد : لأنه قابل الفسخ إذا احتيج إليه حتى يصح هبة المريض و وصيته في جميع ماله في الحال؛ لأنه لا يلحق الضرر بأحد في الحال، وإنما يلحق بالموت، فإذا مات المريض من ذلك المرض يفسخ هبة ووصية بقدر ما يقع به صيانة الحق؛ لأنه حينئذ احتيج إلى فسخه صيانة لحق الغريم والوارث. (السنبلي) إن احتيج إليه بأن كان الموهوب والمحابي في حق الغريم.(القمر)
وما لا يحتمل الفسخ جعل كالمعلق بالموت، وهو المدبر كالإعتاق إذا وقع على حق غريم أي المعلق بالموت أو وارث بأن أعتق عبدًا من ماله المستغرق بالدين، أو أعتق عبدا قيمته تزيد على الثلث، فحكم هذا المعتق: حكم المدبر قبل الموت، فيكون عبدًا في جميع الأحكام المتعلقة بالحرية، أي هذا المعتق، من الكرامات، وبعد الموت يكون حرًا، ويسعى في قيمته للغرماء والورثة، وأما إن كان، أي موت المولى، المال وفاء بالدين، أو هو يخرج من الثلث، فينفذ العتق في الحال لعدم تعلق حق أحد به. أي هذا المعتق بخلاف إعتاق الراهن حيث ينفذ، جواب سؤال مقدر، وهو أنكم قلتم: إن الإعتاق لا ينفذ في الحال إذا وقع على حق غريم أو وارث، ومع ذلك جوزتم إعتاق الراهن عبدا مرهونا يتعلق به حق المرتمن؟ فأجاب بأن إعتاق الراهن إنما ينفذ؛ لأن حق المرتمن في اليد دون الرقبة؛ إذ في الرقبة بقي حق الراهن، وصحة الإعتاق تبتني عليه.
Statement of legal capacity. Statement of illness. So that his marriage, divorce, and all that relates to his utterance are valid. But because death is the cause, and that (the patient) (the illness) (the situation that death is pure inability), illness is from the causes of inability, so acts of worship are prescribed for him (the patient) with possible ability: he prays sitting if unable to stand, and lying if unable to sit. And since death is the cause of succession, i.e., succession of the heir and creditors in his property, illness is from the causes of attachment of the right of the heir and creditor to his property, so it becomes a cause of interdiction to the extent of safeguarding the right (the patient's) i.e., on the patient, the right of the creditor and heir. The patient is interdicted from the amount of the debt which is the right of the creditor, and from two-thirds which is the right of the heir, but not absolutely; rather if it is connected to death and he dies from that illness, then his being interdicted appears, but it is based on its beginning, meaning it is said at death: he was interdicted from disposition from the beginning of the illness, so that the illness does not affect, related to his saying: 'to the extent of safeguarding the right,' meaning the illness only affects what is attached to the right of another, and does not affect what is not attached to the right of a creditor or heir, like marriage with a proper dower, for it is from essential needs, and their right is attached to what is surplus from it, so it is valid immediately. This interdiction is for the preservation of progeny through marriage, the heirs and creditors. Essential needs. Every disposition that admits of rescission, like gift and favoritism, i.e., selling for less than the value; since death is doubtful at the moment, and there is no harm to anyone in the validity of this disposition at the moment, so it should be valid then. It is rescinded if needed, i.e., the deficiency upon the realization of need. Connected to death because the cause of interdiction is a deadly illness, not the illness itself. (al-Qamar) Connected to death because it is not apparent that this is an illness of death except by its connection to death; when it connects, it is established that it is a fatal illness, so interdiction is established based on its beginning; because the cause of interdiction is the fatal illness, so interdiction is attributed to the entire cause from the day of beginning to the day of death. (al-Sunbulī) Harm to anyone: because it admits of rescission if needed, so that the gift and will of the patient are valid in all his property immediately; because there is no harm to anyone immediately, harm only occurs at death. So if the patient dies from that illness, the gift and will are rescinded to the extent that safeguards the right; because then the need to rescind it arises to safeguard the right of the creditor and heir. (al-Sunbulī) It is needed if the gifted and favored are in the right of the creditor. (al-Qamar) And what does not admit of rescission is treated as suspended on death, which is a mudabbar, like manumission if it falls on the right of a creditor (i.e., suspended on death) or an heir, such as emancipating a slave from his property that is completely covered by debt, or emancipating a slave whose value exceeds the one-third. So the ruling of this freed slave is the ruling of a mudabbar before death: he remains a slave in all rulings related to freedom (i.e., this freed slave) like honors, and after death he becomes free and works to pay his value to the creditors and heirs. But if the property (i.e., death of the master) covers the debt, or the slave comes out of the one-third, then the manumission is effective immediately because no one's right is attached to it. This freed slave is unlike the manumission of a pledgor where it is effective. This is an answer to an implied question: you said that manumission is not effective immediately if it falls on the right of a creditor or heir, yet you permitted the pledgor to emancipate a pledged slave to which the pledgee's right is attached? He answered: the pledgor's manumission is effective because the pledgee's right is in the possession (yad) not in the corpus (raqaba); the right of the pledgor remains in the corpus, and the validity of manumission is based on it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining the legal capacity (ahliyya) of a terminally ill person (marad al-mawt) in Hanafi fiqh. The author first states that the ill person's declarations (marriage, divorce) are valid because they depend on utterance, which is sound. However, since illness leads to death, which is a pure inability, the person is obligated to perform acts of worship according to ability (e.g., praying sitting or lying). Then it shifts to financial matters: death transfers property to heirs and creditors, so terminal illness creates a cause for interdiction (hajar) to protect their rights. The patient is interdicted from disposing of amounts exceeding one-third of his estate or debts, but this interdiction is not immediate; it only takes effect if the illness connects to death and he dies from it, retroactively from the start of the illness. The illness only affects dispositions that involve others' rights; essential needs like marriage with a proper dower are valid immediately because they serve a fundamental need (procreation) and creditors only have claim on surplus. For transactions that can be rescinded (e.g., gifts, sales below value), they are valid initially but rescinded after death to protect creditors/heirs. For acts that cannot be rescinded (e.g., manumission), they are treated like a mudabbar (slave freed upon death) — the slave is free only after death and must work to pay value to claimants. A distinction is made with a pledgor's manumission: a mortgaged slave can be freed by the pledgor because the pledgee's right is only in possession, not the corpus; thus manumission is valid immediately. The reasoning uses the underlying cause (illa) that the fatal illness as a whole causes interdiction, not the illness in its early stages.

Key Terms
المرض (al-marad): المرض المميت الذي يتصل بالموت فيثبت به الحجر على المريض في التصرفات المالية
الحجر (al-hajar): المنع من التصرف في المال بسبب المرض المخوف حفاظا على حق الغرماء والورثة
المعلق بالموت (al-mu'allaq bi al-mawt): تصرف لا يحتمل الفسخ كالعتق والتدبير يجعل كالمعلق على الموت فلا ينفذ إلا بعد الوفاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be met for the legal restriction (hajar) on a terminally ill person's transactions to take effect?
A) The illness must last more than a year
B) The illness must be connected to death and the person dies from it
C) The person must be unconscious
D) The person must have no relatives

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule of interdiction for the terminally ill is conditional upon death from that illness, protecting the rights of creditors and heirs while preserving essential needs and allowing rescindable transactions to be adjusted post-mortem.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Menstruation and Postpartum: Loss of Condition and Lapse of Obligation
Explains why hayd/nifas prevent prayer without qada despite full capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that menstruation and postpartum bleeding remove the obligation of prayer without requiring qada, applying the principle that loss of a permanent condition voids the obligation.

Core Idea
When a condition for an act is permanently absent, the obligation itself lapses.

In the previous lesson, we saw that illness is an excuse that delays prayer but does not remove the obligation of qada. This lesson turns to menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas). Though these conditions do not strip a woman of her legal capacity, she is not required to pray or fast, nor must she make them up. The key lies in understanding the role of purification (tahara) as a condition (shart) for prayer. When the condition is permanently unavailable, the obligation to perform the act disappears.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
Prayer requires ritual purity (tahara) from major and minor impurities. Menstruation and postpartum bleeding prevent this purification.
The condition is permanent for the duration of hayd/nifas.
2
Confirm that legal capacity remains intact
The text states that hayd and nifas do not remove ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for obligation) nor ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity for performance). The woman remains a mukallafa.
If capacity were lost, the obligation would be removed entirely, but here the reasoning is different.
3
Apply the rule: absence of condition voids performance
Since purification is a condition, its absence makes performance (ada') impossible. When performance is impossible, the ruling of obligation (hukm al-wujub) cannot be fulfilled and thus lapses.
The obligation is not merely deferred; it is voided for that time.
4
Conclude: no qada is required
Because the obligation never effectively existed during the period of hayd/nifas, there is no missed duty to make up.
This differs from illness, where purification is still possible, so qada remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحيض والنفاس معطوف على ما قبله ذكرهما بعد المرض لاتصالهما به من حيث كونهما عذرًا. أي قوله الصغر أي الحيض والنفاس وهما لا يعدمان الأهلية لا أهلية الوجوب ولا أهلية الأداء، فكان ينبغي أن لا تسقط بهما الصلاة والصوم، لكن الطهارة عنهما للصلاة شرط، وفي فوت الشرط يفوت الأداء، أي عن الحيض والنفاس، جعل كالمعلق أي في حق السعاية، ولا يجعل هذا صحيحًا في الحال؛ لأنه لا يمكن نقضه، ففي القول بصحته في الحال ضرر لصاحب الحق. (القمر) والورثة: أي هذا الحكم إذا لم يخرج العبد من الثلث أو لم يكن في المال وفاء بالدين. (المحشي دون الرقبة بخلاف حق الوارث والغريم، فإنه يتعلق بالرقبة (القمر) تبتني عليه أي على ملك الرقبة دون اليد ألا ترى أن إعتاق الآبق صحيح مع زوال ملك اليد. (القمر) والنفاس جمعهما لتشابههما صورة وحكمًا. وهما لا يعدمان إلخ لبقاء الذمة والتميز وقدرة البدن.(القمر) الصلاة والصوم لأنهما لا يخلان بالذمة والعقل والقدرة البدنية (السنبلي لكن الطهارة إلخ: هذا دفع لوهم وهو: أنه على هذا المذكور من عدم إعدامها الأهلية ينبغي أن لا يسقط بهما القضاء للصلاة. (السنبلي) يفوت الأداء: وهو حكم الوجوب، فإذا خلا الوجوب عن حكمه لغا؛ وفات الوجوب أيضا، فلا يجب القضاء. (القمر)
And menstruation and postpartum bleeding are conjoined to what preceded; they are mentioned after illness because of their connection to it in terms of being excuses. His saying (the author's) 'minority' means menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The two do not remove legal capacity, neither capacity for obligation nor capacity for performance, so it would seem that prayer and fasting should not lapse due to them. But purification from them for prayer is a condition, and when the condition is lost, the performance is lost. They are treated as 'suspended' like in the case of 'pursuit' (sa'aya), and this is not made valid immediately because it cannot be rescinded, so saying it is valid immediately harms the right-holder. (al-Qamar) And the heirs: this ruling if the slave does not come out of the one-third or the property does not cover the debt. (al-Muhashshi) Without the corpus unlike the right of the heir and creditor, for it is attached to the corpus. (al-Qamar) It is based on it, i.e., on ownership of the corpus without the possession; do you not see that the manumission of an absconded slave is valid despite the loss of possession? (al-Qamar) And postpartum bleeding: they are grouped together due to their similarity in form and ruling. The two do not remove [capacity] etc. because of the remaining of the dhimma (legal personality), discernment, and bodily ability. (al-Qamar) Prayer and fasting, because they do not void the dhimma, intellect, and bodily ability. (al-Sunbulī) But purification etc.: this repels a misunderstanding, which is: based on this mentioned lack of removing capacity, it would seem that qada (making up) for prayers should not lapse. (al-Sunbulī) Performance lapses: it is the ruling of obligation; when the obligation is devoid of its ruling, it becomes void; and the obligation also passes, so qada is not obligatory. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal status of menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) as excuses preventing prayer and fasting. The author first links them to the preceding discussion of illness because all three are excuses (udhr). The key point is that menstruation and postpartum do not deprive a woman of legal capacity (ahliyya) — she remains capable of obligation (ahliyyat wujub) and performance (ahliyyat ada'). Therefore, one might think that prayer and fasting should still be obligatory upon her. However, the ruling is that they are not obligatory, and no qada is required. The reason is that purification from these states is a condition (shart) for the validity of prayer. Since the condition is absent, the performance (ada') of the obligation is not possible. When performance is impossible, the obligation itself lapses (yafut al-wujub). Thus, there is no requirement to make up the missed prayers. The commentary notes that this is similar to the earlier case of 'suspended' (mu'allaq) transactions: the obligation is treated as suspended, not effective immediately, because if it were effective immediately, it would cause harm (since it cannot be fulfilled). The glosses add that the capacity remains due to the continued existence of dhimma, discernment, and physical ability. The misunderstanding addressed is: if capacity remains, why doesn't qada become required? The answer is that when the condition for performance is permanently absent (the entire period), the obligation itself is voided, so no qada.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): دم يخرج من فرج المرأة في أوقات معلومة ليس بسبب الولادة
النفاس (al-nifas): دم يخرج من المرأة بعد الولادة
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): صلاحية الشخص لوجوب الحقوق عليه وأدائها
الشرط (al-shart): ما يتوقف عليه وجود الحكم ولا يلزم منه وجود الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does menstruation prevent the obligation of prayer without requiring qada, according to this passage?
A) Because the woman loses her legal capacity entirely during menstruation
B) Because purification is a condition for prayer, and its permanent loss causes the performance and thus the obligation to lapse
C) Because menstruation is a punishment
D) Because prayer is only obligatory for men

Key Takeaway
Menstruation and postpartum do not remove legal capacity, but they prevent prayer because purification is a condition; since the condition is permanently absent, the obligation itself lapses, so no qada is required.
